According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and it is a narration from him: she separates without separation because the law gave her choice at the completion of the year. His statement 'if she requests' refers to all, meaning separation, deferment, and final separation. The request of her agent for separation in her absence is like her request. Then the separation is a irrevocable divorce according to us, while according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad it is annulment because it is a separation from her side. And for us: this separation is from his side because the obligation on him is retention, so when it fails, release is obligatory. So when he refrained, the judge took his place, so the act is attributed to him, so it is an irrevocable divorce to realize the removal of oppression from her. The valid, effective, and binding marriage does not admit annulment. By separation: meaning by separation of the husband and the ruler. If the woman requests separation because it is her right, and this if she is free; if she is a slave, then the request belongs to her master.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the impotent husband combined with an intra-Hanafi dispute. It begins with the main ruling: if an impotent husband (or eunuch) has intercourse within one year, the deferral period ends; if he does not, the wife is separated by the judge's decree if she requests. The commentary explains that the one-year period is established by the consensus of the Companions. If the husband consummates even once, her right to separation is fulfilled; additional intercourse is religiously incumbent but not legally required. Then the intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abu Hanifa holds that separation requires the judge's decree (tafriq) when the husband refuses to divorce. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) hold that separation occurs automatically after one year without the judge, because the law gave her the choice at that point. The phrase 'if she requests' is interpreted by Abu Hanifa to apply to all aspects: deferral, separation, and finality. The passage also addresses the inter-madhhab difference: according to Hanafis, the separation is an irrevocable divorce (talaq bain), while Shafi'i and Ahmad consider it an annulment (faskh). The Hanafi proof is that the marriage is valid and binding, and the judge stands in the husband's place to effect a divorce, so it is attributed to the husband, making it a talaq. The ruling concludes by stating that the free woman requests separation; for a slave, the master requests.

Key Terms
الطلاق البائن (al-talaq al-ba’in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; the husband cannot return during iddat without a new contract.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Annulment of a contract, here the marriage, often retroactive or without counting as a divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key practical difference between the Hanafi ruling (talaq bain) and the Shafi'i ruling (faskh) for separation due to impotence?
A) The wife must observe iddat only in faskh.
B) The separation counts as a divorce only in talaq bain.
C) The husband can return without a new contract only in talaq bain.
D) There is no practical difference.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school regards separation for impotence as talaq bain, not faskh, because the judge acts on behalf of the husband, attributing the divorce to him.

Resolving Disputes of Consummation: Virgin vs Non-Virgin
Explains the procedure when husband claims consummation and wife denies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi procedural ruling for a virgin wife versus a non-virgin wife when the husband claims consummation after the one-year deferral, and apply the evidentiary principles to determine which party bears the burden of proof.

Core Idea
Virginity proves no intercourse; non-virginity is ambiguous, so the husband's oath prevails.

When a husband claims he has consummated the marriage after the year of waiting for impotence (‘inan), and the wife denies, Hanafi law uses physical inspection to resolve the dispute. The procedure differs sharply based on whether the wife is found to be a virgin (bikr) or non-virgin (thayyib). This lesson unpacks the evidentiary logic and procedural steps from Kanz al-Daqā’iq, showing how the default presumptions shift the burden of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factual scenarios
The text distinguishes between a wife found to be a virgin (bikr) and one found to be non-virgin (thayyib). The ruling differs based on this physical evidence.
Non-virginity may result from intercourse or other causes, creating ambiguity.
2
Trace the procedure for the virgin case
If women inspectors declare the wife is a virgin, she is given an immediate choice to stay or separate. If she chooses separation, the judge orders the husband to divorce; if he refuses, the judge separates them.
Choice is exercised in the same sitting (majlis).
3
Analyse the evidentiary basis for virginity
The testimony of one trustworthy woman is sufficient, and two is more precautionary. Their testimony supports the default presumption that virginity is the original state (asl) in women. Thus, the wife’s claim is strengthened.
The text says: 'their testimony supports what is already supported' – virginity is inherent.
4
Compare with the non-virgin case
If the wife is found to be non-virgin, the husband is believed with his oath. The reason is that non-virginity does not necessarily prove he had intercourse; it could have been lost by another cause. The analogue is the depositor who claims to have returned the deposit – his word is taken over the claimant’s.
The husband is a 'denier in meaning' (munkir ma’nan) regarding the wife’s right to separation.
5
Synthesize the ruling
The text establishes a clear evidentiary hierarchy: when physical evidence shows virginity, the wife’s claim is accepted and she gets the choice; when it shows non-virginity, the husband’s oath is accepted because the fact of non-virginity does not confirm consummation.
This is based on the maxim: 'Consideration is given to meaning, not form.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قال: أي الزوج بعد تمام السنة. (ط) وقلن بكر خيرت [ أي المرأة لثبوت حقها في مجلسها. (ط، ع)] أي إذا أجل العنين سنة، وقال: قد جامعتها، وأنكرت، نظر إليها النساء، فإن قلن: هي "بكر" خيرت المرأة في الحال بين الإقامة والفرقة في مجلسها، فإن . اختارت نفسها أمره القاضي بالتطليق، فإن أبى فرق بينهما، و"قلن" بصيغة الجمع لبيان الأولى، ويكتفي بقول امرأة ثقة، وقول امرأتين ،أحوط وخيرت بشهادتهن؛ لأن شهادتهن تأييد لمؤيد، وهي البكارة؛ إذ البكارة أصل فيهن، فيثبت بقولهن. (مستخلص، فتح) صدق بحلفه: أي إن كانت ثيباً في الأصل فالقول قوله مع اليمين؛ لأنه منكر معنى لثبوت حقها بالفرقة، وإن منكرة للوصول صورة؛ إذ العبرة للمعنى لا للصورة كالمودع إذا ادعى رد الوديعة فالقول قوله؛ لأن الثيابة ثبتت ،بقولهن وليس من ضرورة ثبوت الثيابة الوصول إليها؛ لاحتمال زوالها بشيء آخر، فيحلف، بخلاف البكارة؛ لأن ثبوتها ينفي الوصول إليها ضرورة فتخير بقولهن. (مستخلص، عيني) كانت هي
If he says: meaning the husband after the completion of the year. And they (the women) say 'virgin', she is given choice: meaning the woman due to the establishment of her right in her sitting. Meaning when the impotent man is given a year, and he says 'I have had intercourse with her' and she denies, the women inspect her. If they say she is a virgin, the woman is given a choice in the moment between staying and separation in her sitting. Then if she chooses herself, the judge orders him to divorce, and if he refuses, he separates between them. 'They say' in plural form indicates the best method, but the statement of one trustworthy woman is sufficient, and the statement of two women is more precautionary. She is given choice based on their testimony because their testimony supports what is already supported, which is virginity. Since virginity is inherent in them, it is established by their statement. He is believed with his oath: meaning if she is originally non-virgin, then the statement is his with the oath because he is expressing denial in meaning regarding the establishment of her right to separation, while she is denying the actual occurrence in form. Consideration goes to meaning, not form, like the depositor when he claims return of the deposit, his statement is accepted. Because non-virginity is established by their statement, but it does not necessarily imply that access occurred, as it could have been lost by something else. So he takes an oath, contrary to virginity, because its establishment necessarily negates access, so she is given choice based on their statement.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage provides the procedure when the husband claims he has consummated the marriage after the one-year deferral, and the wife denies. The women inspect the wife. If they declare her a virgin, she is given a choice immediately in the same sitting: to stay with her husband or to seek separation. The judge then orders the husband to divorce; if he refuses, the judge separates them. The inspection can be conducted by one trustworthy woman, though two is better. If the wife is found to be non-virgin (thayyib), the husband's statement is accepted with his oath, because non-virginity does not necessarily prove that he had intercourse; she could have lost her virginity by other means. This is analogous to a depositor who claims he returned the deposit—his word is taken. In contrast, a virgin finding proves that no intercourse occurred, so her statement is supported. Thus, the procedure distinguishes between the two states: virginity gives the wife the option, while non-virginity favors the husband's claim.

Key Terms
البكارة (al-bakārah): The state of being a virgin, i.e., the hymen is intact as evidence that intercourse has not occurred.
الثيابة (al-thiyābah): The state of being non-virgin, which may result from intercourse or other causes.
التخيير (al-takhyīr): The giving of a choice to the woman between staying with the husband or seeking separation.
العنين (al-‘inīn): A man who is impotent and unable to consummate the marriage.
اليمين (al-yamīn): An oath sworn by the husband that he did consummate, which is accepted in case the wife is non-virgin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The husband is required to produce witnesses.
C) The husband's statement is believed with his oath.
D) The judge automatically grants separation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi evidentiary procedure for a disputed consummation claim hinges on the wife's physical state: virginity gives her the choice, while non-virginity shifts the burden to the husband's oath.

No Option for Defects: Hanafi Rule and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on options for defects, with Hanafi rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on options due to defects, explain the Hanafi critique of the Shafi'i proofs, and compare the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) between the schools.

Core Idea
Marriage is not annulled by defects; only divorce, not faskh, is the remedy.

In this passage, the author first explains that if a wife chooses to stay with an impotent husband, her right to separation lapses permanently. Then, the text addresses a broader dispute: whether a spouse can annul the marriage due to severe defects in the other. Many schools grant such an option, but the Hanafi school stands firm that the marriage remains valid and the only recourse is divorce. Let us examine the Hanafi reasoning and its rebuttals to the opposing proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on defects
State clearly that the Hanafi school does not grant either spouse an option to annul the marriage due to defects like leprosy, madness, ratq, or qarn. The marriage continues despite these defects.
The only exception is confirmed impotence, which is handled separately with a one-year deferral.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proofs
The Shafi'i (and Malik, Ahmad) school grants an option for annulment (faskh) based on three proofs: a hadith of the Prophet returning a woman with vitiligo, the saying of Ali 'Flee from the leper', and an analogy with sale where a defective item can be returned.
These proofs are considered by the Hanafi school to be either weak or misinterpreted.
3
Analyze the Hanafi critique of each proof
The Hanafi response: (1) The hadith is weak due to an unreliable chain (Jamil ibn Zaid is abandoned, Zaid is unknown). (2) Ali's statement only recommends flight, not a legal option for annulment; its apparent meaning is not intended by consensus because one may be rewarded for serving a leper. (3) The sale analogy fails because marriage is not a pure exchange contract; the primary right is intercourse, which is not completely prevented by these defects, only impaired.
The Hanafis emphasize that the right established by marriage is the ability to have intercourse, not a guarantee of the partner's physical perfection.
4
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The key difference: Under Shafi'i, a spouse can go to court and obtain an annulment (faskh) due to these defects. Under Hanafi, no annulment is possible; the spouse must seek divorce if they wish to end the marriage.
This ikhtilaf has real implications for spouses seeking relief from severe hereditary or contagious conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اختارته بطل حقها، ولم يخير أحدهما بعيب. أي أحد الزوجين وإن اختارته بطل حقها [ولا يكون لها خيار، وكذا لو وطئها مرة ثم عجز. (ط)] أي إن اختارت زوجها لم يكن لها بعد ذلك خيار؛ لأن المخير بين شيئين لا يكون له إلا أحدهما، وكذا إذا قامت من مجلسها، أو أقامها أعوان القاضي قبل أن تختار شيئاً؛ لأنها رضيت ببطلان حقها، وإن اختارت الفرقة أمر القاضي الزوج أن يطلقها بائنة، فإن أبى فرق بينهما، وقيل: تقع الفرقة باختيارها نفسها، ولا يحتاج إلى القضاء كخيار العتق، ولو فرق بينهما ثم تزوجها ثانياً لم يكن لها اختيار رمز الحقائق: ٢٩٤/١] (مستخلص) ولم يخير أحدهما بعيب : [في الآخر ولو فاحشا بجنون وجذام وبرص ورتق وقرن. (ط)] وقال الشافعي: له أن يردها بالجنون والجذام والبرص والرتق والقرن؛ لأنها تمنع الاستيفاء حساً أو طبعاً، والطبع مؤيد بالشرع، قال علي: "فر من المجذوم فرارك من الأسد" وردّ رسول الله الله بالبرص وقال: "الحقي بأهلك" حين وجد بكشحها وضحاً أو بياضاً؛ ولأن النكاح يشبه البيع؛ لأنه عقد مبادلة والمبيع يرد بالعيب وبه قال مالك وأحمد. ولنا: أن المستحق بالعقد هو الوطء، وهذه العيوب لا تفوته بل توجب فيه خللاً ففواته بالهلاك قبل التسليم يوجب الفسخ فاختلاله أولى أن لا يوجب، وما رواه لا يصح؛ لأنه من رواية جميل بن زيد، وهو متروك عن زيد بن كعب بن عجرة، وهو مجهول لا يعلم لكعب ولد اسمه زيد ولا حجة له في قوله عليها: "فر من المجذوم"؛ لأنه يوجب الفرار لا الخيار، وظاهره ليس بمراد إجماعاً؛ لأنه يجوز أن يدنو منه ويثاب على خدمته. لا رمز الحقائق: ٢٩٤/١] (فتح)
If she chooses him, her right lapses, and neither of them is given option due to a defect. Meaning either of the spouses. If she chooses her husband, thereafter she has no option, similarly if he had intercourse once then became incapable. If she chooses her husband, she has no option thereafter because the one given choice between two things can only have one. Similarly if she leaves her sitting, or the judge's officers remove her before she chooses anything, because she has consented to the lapse of her right. If she chooses separation, the judge orders the husband to divorce her irrevocably; if he refuses, he separates between them. And it is said: the separation occurs by her choice itself, and he has no need for a ruling, like the option of emancipation. If he separates between them then marries her again, she has no option. And neither is given option due to a defect in the other, even if it is loathsome: madness, leprosy, vitiligo, vaginal occlusion, or a bone. And al-Shafi'i said: He has the right to return her due to madness, leprosy, vitiligo, vaginal occlusion, and a bone, because they prevent fulfillment either physically or naturally, and nature is supported by law. Ali said: 'Flee from the leper as you flee from a lion.' The Messenger of God returned a woman due to vitiligo and said: 'Go to your family' when he found a white spot on her side. And because marriage resembles a sale; it is a contract of exchange and the sold item is returned due to defect. Malik and Ahmad said likewise. And for us: The right established by the contract is intercourse, and these defects do not negate it but cause deficiency in it. So if its total loss (by death before delivery) necessitates annulment, then its deficiency is more appropriate not to necessitate annulment. And what he narrated is not authentic because it is from the narration of Jamil ibn Zaid, who is abandoned, from Zaid ibn Ka'b ibn 'Ujrah, who is unknown; no son of Ka'b named Zaid is known. And there is no proof in his statement 'Flee from the leper' because it implies fleeing, not option, and its apparent meaning is not intended by consensus, because it is permissible to approach him and be rewarded for serving him.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage covers two topics: first, the consequence when the wife chooses to remain with the impotent husband—her right to separation lapses permanently, and she cannot revisit it even if he later becomes incapacitated. Leaving the sitting or being removed by the judge's officers also nullifies her right. However, if she chooses separation, the judge enforces it; some say it occurs by her choice alone, like the option of emancipation. Second, the passage addresses the inter-madhhab dispute about defects. According to the Hanafi school, neither spouse has an option to annul the marriage due to a defect in the other, even severe ones like madness, leprosy, vitiligo, vaginal occlusion (ratq) or a bone growth (qarn). The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools grant an option for such defects because they prevent the purpose of marriage (intercourse) either physically or naturally, and they cite practices of the Prophet and Ali. The Hanafi response is that the essential right in marriage is intercourse itself, and these defects do not eliminate it—only create difficulty. Total loss (like death before consummation) would justify annulment, but mere deficiency does not. They also critique the Shafi'i proofs: the narration from Ali is authentic but only recommends flight, not a legal option; the hadith of the Prophet returning a woman with vitiligo is weak due to a defective chain and, even if sound, the command was to return her to her family, not to nullify the marriage. Thus, the Hanafi position is that no option exists due to defects.

Key Terms
الرتق (al-ratq): Vaginal occlusion (the woman's vagina is closed, preventing intercourse).
القرن (al-qarn): A bone growth in the vagina that impedes intercourse.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Annulment of a contract, here marriage, due to a defect or impediment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only the husband has the option.
C) Neither spouse has the option.
D) The option is given only if the defect prevents intercourse entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not grant an option to annul for defects other than absolute impotence; the marriage remains valid and divorce is the only remedy.

No Right to Annulment for Defects in Marriage: Hanafi Position vs. Other Schools
Inter-madhhab dispute on defects and annulment in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that neither spouse can annul the marriage due to severe defects, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using hadith and analogy.

Core Idea
Severe defects in a spouse do not give the right to annul the marriage; only judicial divorce is possible.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a key difference between the Hanafi school and other madhahib: whether a spouse can annul the marriage due to severe defects like insanity, leprosy, or vitiligo. The Hanafi school maintains that the marriage remains valid and the wife can only seek separation through a judge, while the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools grant a right of annulment. We will examine the proofs of each side and understand the reasoning behind the Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on defects
Identify the ruling: neither spouse has a right to annul due to defects; only judicial divorce is available.
The passage states 'لم يخير أحدهما بعيب' — 'neither is given a choice due to a defect'.
2
Identify the opposing position and its evidence
Imam al-Shafi'i and others allow annulment for severe defects like insanity, leprosy, etc. Their evidence includes the hadith 'Flee from the leper' and analogy to sale contracts.
Opposing school: Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the opposing evidence
Hanafi scholars critique the hadith as weak (Jamil ibn Zayd matruk, Zayd ibn Ka'b majhool) and argue that the command to flee does not create a legal option. They also reject the analogy to sale because marriage is not purely an exchange contract.
4
Understand the Hanafi's own proof
The Hanafi school argues that the primary right in marriage is intercourse, and defects only cause deficiency, not total loss, so annulment is not justified. If complete loss (death) necessitates rescission, then mere deficiency should not.
This is a rational argument based on the nature of the marital contract.
5
Recognise the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi law, the wife must seek judicial divorce; in Shafi'i law, she can annul the marriage herself (faskh). This affects the legal procedure and validity of self-annulment.

Source Text

Chapter of the Impotent Man: And if she chooses him, her right becomes void, and neither of the two spouses is given a choice due to a defect. Meaning either of the spouses. And if she chooses him, her right becomes void [and she has no option, and likewise if he had intercourse with her once then became incapable. (T)] i.e. if she chooses her husband, she has no option after that; because the one given a choice between two things only gets one of them, and likewise if she rises from her session, or the judge's assistants make her stand before she chooses anything; because she has consented to the nullification of her right. And if she chooses separation, the judge orders the husband to divorce her irrevocably, and if he refuses, he separates them. And it is said: the separation occurs by her choosing herself, and there is no need for a judicial decree, like the option of emancipation. And if he separates them and then marries her again, she has no option. ... And he said: neither of them is given a choice due to a defect: [in the other, even if it is severe, like insanity, leprosy, vitiligo, vaginal occlusion, and a fleshy growth. (T)] And al-Shafi'i said: he has the right to return her (annul) due to insanity, leprosy, vitiligo, occlusion, and growth; because they prevent fulfillment either sensually or naturally, and nature is supported by the Law; 'Ali said: 'Flee from the leper as you flee from a lion.' And the Messenger of Allah returned a woman due to vitiligo and said: 'Go back to your family' when he found a spot or whiteness on her side. And because marriage resembles sale, as it is a contract of exchange, and the sold item is returned due to a defect; and Malik and Ahmad said the same. And our proof: The right due to the contract is intercourse, and these defects do not prevent it but cause deficiency in it. The loss of the object (intercourse) by destruction before delivery necessitates rescission, so its deficiency is more entitled not to necessitate it. And what he narrated is not authentic, because it is from the narration of Jamil ibn Zayd, who is abandoned, from Zayd ibn Ka'b ibn 'Ujrah, who is unknown; it is not known that Ka'b had a son named Zayd. And there is no proof in his statement: 'Flee from the leper' because it necessitates flight, not option, and its apparent meaning is not intended by consensus, because he may approach him and be rewarded for serving him. No.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the right of a wife to annul her marriage due to severe defects in the husband, such as insanity, leprosy, vitiligo, vaginal occlusion (a fleshy growth), and other similar defects. The chapter begins by stating that if a wife, who has the option to stay or leave due to her husband's impotence, chooses to stay, her right to separation is void. Then it addresses a general rule: neither spouse is given a choice to annul the marriage due to a defect in the other. The commentary explains that the wife has no option if she chooses the husband or if she leaves the session without making a choice. If she chooses separation, the judge orders an irrevocable divorce; if the husband refuses, the judge separates them. Some say the separation occurs by her choice alone, like in the case of an option upon emancipation. The passage then presents the Shafi'i position, which grants the wife the right to annul the marriage due to the listed defects. Their evidence includes hadith about fleeing from the leper, the Prophet returning a woman due to a spot, and analogy to defects in sale contracts. The Hanafi response argues that the primary right in marriage is intercourse, and these defects only cause deficiency, not total loss, so annulment is not justified. The hadith cited are weak due to unreliable narrators, and the command to flee does not establish a legal option. Thus, the Hanafi school maintains that the marriage remains valid and the wife cannot unilaterally annul it. The relied-upon position is that the wife must seek judicial divorce if she cannot live with the defect.

Key Terms
العنن (al-'unna): Impotence of the husband, a condition that gives the wife an option to seek separation.
عيب (‘ayb): A defect in a spouse that could potentially give the other a right to annul the marriage; in Hanafi law, severe defects do not give such a right.
الفرقة (al-furqah): Separation between spouses, either by divorce or judicial decree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a woman discovers after marriage that her husband has leprosy, what is her legal recourse?
A) She has the right to immediately annul the marriage (faskh).
B) She may seek a judicial divorce if she cannot live with him, but she cannot annul the marriage herself.
C) She must remain in the marriage with no option.
D) She can claim the dower and leave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, no spouse can annul the marriage due to defects; the only recourse is judicial divorce.

The Meaning of 'Qur' in the 'Iddah: A Hanafi Perspective
Definition and inter-madhhab dispute on 'iddah and qur'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi interpretation of 'qur' in the waiting period and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using textual and linguistic evidence.

Core Idea
For the Hanafi school, 'qur' means menstruation — the 'iddah is three complete menstrual cycles.

After divorce, a woman must observe a waiting period called 'iddah. But how long? The Quran says 'three qur',' but what does 'qur' mean? The Hanafi school, following the teachings of Imam Abu Hanifa and his students, interprets 'qur' as menstruation (hayd). This lesson examines the legal definition of 'iddah and the key dispute with the Shafi'i and Maliki schools, providing the Hanafi proofs and rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Define 'iddah and identify its elements
Explain the legal definition of 'iddah, its cause (marriage confirmed by consummation), condition (separation), pillar (prohibitions), and nature as a right of Allah.
This sets the foundation for the specific dispute.
2
State the Hanafi position on 'qur'
The Hanafi school holds that 'qur' means menstruation (hayd). Therefore, the waiting period for a free woman after a consummated divorce is three menstrual periods.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
3
Identify the opposing view and its proof
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools interpret 'qur' as purity (tuhr). Their main proofs are the hadith of Ibn 'Umar and a grammatical argument about the feminine 'ta' in 'thalathata'.
Understanding the opponent's strongest evidence is crucial for tarjih.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing proof
The Hanafi response includes: (1) The three qur' must be complete; purities would often be two and part of a third. (2) The verse about months shows menstruation is the basis. (3) The hadith that a slave woman's 'iddah is two menstruations implies a free woman's is three. The hadith of Ibn 'Umar is about the time of divorce, not the length of 'iddah.
The Hanafi response uses both tafsir and usul al-fiqh arguments.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Hanafi view, 'iddah ends after the third menstrual period; under the Shafi'i view, after the third pure interval. This affects the exact date when the woman becomes free to remarry.
This difference can impact the validity of subsequent marriages and paternity.

Chapter of the Waiting Period: Meaning explaining its rulings. It is a waiting period that is binding upon the woman; and the waiting period of a free woman for divorce or annulment is three qur' i.e., menstruations. (Waiting period). And even if she is a kitabiyya under a Muslim, whether the divorce is irrevocable or revocable. Interpretation of aqra': They are... Chapter of the 'Iddah: [Linguistically, it is a masdar from 'adda ya'uddu: you say 'adadtu bishay'in i.e. I counted it. (A)] with a kasra it means counting, with a damma it means extension for a matter. R. Legally: waiting a known period that becomes binding upon the woman after the dissolution of marriage or its strong semblance confirmed by actual or legal intercourse, or death. Its cause according to us: marriage confirmed by taslim (delivery) or what runs in its course. Its condition: separation. Its pillar: prohibitions established by it, like marrying her sister or four others besides her. And it is a right of Allah; therefore it is not dropped if she drops it; it is not permissible for her to go out even if her husband permits her. And the two waiting periods overlap, and the right of Allah does not overlap. And he placed the waiting period after the types of separation: divorce, ila', khul', li'an, and rulings of the impotent, because the waiting period is the effect of divorce, and the effect follows the cause. (Fath) The woman: upon the dissolution of marriage, or its semblance, or the bed, so includes umm al-walad. (T) Or annulment other than divorce, like separation due to option of puberty, or one spouse owning the other, apostasy, or lack of equality. (T, A) Three qur' i.e., menstruations [if she is one who menstruates, and the separation occurred after consummation. (A)] Since aqra' is a homonym between menstruation and purity, and the intended is the first, he explained by saying 'i.e., menstruation' and he did not say at the beginning 'three menstruations' following the text. According to us, aqra' are menstruations, and Ahmad said so according to the most correct. According to al-Shafi'i and Malik, they are purities. And Ibn Hanbal used to say that and then recanted (or adopted their view). They have the hadith of Ibn 'Umar that the Messenger of Allah ordered him to take her back, then leave her until she purifies, then divorce her, and then said: 'That is the 'iddah which Allah commanded that women be divorced for.' This is a clear text that the 'iddah is purity. And because the masculine form of 'three' with the addition of the feminine ta' is evidence that the object is masculine (purity), for if the intended were menstruation, it would have been said 'thalathu quru'' without ta', because its singular is feminine (haydah). Our scholars favored the interpretation of qur' as menstruation by three aspects: One: Allah Almighty textually mentioned 'three' and the plural in His words 'wa thalathatu quru'in' (2:228). 'Three' is a name for a known number, it is not permissible to apply it to more or less; applying it to purities leads to applying it to the least, which is two purities and part of a third as is their madhhab, while the complete plural is three, and it is a reality in that, so it is more appropriate. And it cannot be said: it is permissible to apply the word 'plural' to two names and part of a third, like His Almighty's words 'al-hajju ashhurun ma'lumat' (2:197); because we say: that is in the plural devoid of number; as for number and plural together, it is not. Second: Allah Almighty made the counting of months the counting of aqra' in His words 'wa alla'i ya'isna mina al-mahidi min nisa'ikum in irtabtum fa'iddatuhuna thalathatu ashhurin' (65:4). So He made months upon absence of menstruation, indicating that it (menstruation) is the basis. Third: the statement of 'Ali: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstruations.' This indicates that the waiting period of a free woman is three menstruations. And the hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud from 'A'ishah marfu'an. And the slave woman is not different from the free woman... instead of...
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a definition and inter-madhhab dispute regarding the meaning of 'iddah and the term 'qur'' (plural of qur'). The chapter defines 'iddah linguistically and legally, explaining that it is a mandatory waiting period for a woman after the dissolution of marriage, whether by divorce, annulment, or death. The cause of obligation is marriage confirmed by consummation or its equivalent. Its condition is separation, and its pillar is that certain prohibitions become binding (e.g., cannot remarry the husband or others during this period). It is a right of Allah and cannot be waived. The passage then focuses on the waiting period for a free woman divorced after consummation: three qur'. The Hanafi position, which is the relied-upon view, interprets 'qur'' as menstruations (hayd). The competing view of al-Shafi'i and Malik is that 'qur'' means purities (tuhr). The text provides three Hanafi arguments: first, the Quranic expression 'three qur'' indicates a complete number, and if it meant purities, it would often be two purities and part of a third, which is not a complete number. The analogy with 'al-hajju ashhurun ma'lumat' is rebutted because that phrase lacks a specific number. Second, the verse comparing months for those without menstruation shows that the basis of 'iddah is menstruation. Third, the hadith that the 'iddah of a slave woman is two menstruations implies that a free woman's is three by analogy. The Shafi'i argument from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar is countered by explaining that the hadith is about the timing of divorce, not the length of the 'iddah. Thus, the Hanafi school firmly holds that the 'iddah of a free woman is three menstrual periods.

Key Terms
العدة (al-'iddah): The mandatory waiting period observed by a woman after the dissolution of marriage or after the death of her husband, during which she cannot remarry.
الأقراء (al-aqra'): Plural of qur'; refers to menstruations (hayd) in the Hanafi school, in the context of the waiting period.
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstruation; the Hanafi school holds that the waiting period for a divorced woman is three menstrual cycles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many menstrual periods must a free woman observe for her 'iddah after a divorce that was consummated?
A) Two menstrual periods
B) Three menstrual periods
C) Three pure periods (tuhr)
D) Four months and ten days

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the 'iddah of a free woman after a consummated divorce is three complete menstrual periods, based on the Quranic term 'qur' meaning menstruation.

The Meaning of Quru' in 'Iddah: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Maliki
Defends the Hanafi interpretation of 'quru'' as menstruation with three proofs and refutes Shafi'i/Maliki arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the meaning of 'quru'' in 'iddah, explain the three Hanafi proofs, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-arguments.

Core Idea
One word (quru') divides madhhabs: is it menstruation or purity? The answer changes the 'iddah count.

This passage dives into a classic inter-madhhab dispute over a single Quranic term: 'aqra''. The Hanafi school insists it means menstruations, while the Shafi'i and Maliki schools interpret it as clean periods. The commentator not only states the Hanafi position but systematically refutes the opposing evidence, showing how a single ambiguous word can lead to significant practical differences in family law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school says about the meaning of 'aqra'' in 'iddah. Note the immediate interpretation in the text: 'ثلاثة أقراء أي حيض'.
2
Identify the opposing school and their evidence
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools hold that 'aqra'' means purities (tuhr). Locate their two main proofs: the hadith of Ibn 'Umar and the grammatical argument about the number three.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the hadith
The Hanafi response argues that the hadith of Ibn 'Umar is about the timing of divorce, not the definition of 'iddah. Understand why this refutation works.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the grammatical argument
The Hanafis counter that the word 'qur'' is masculine, so the masculine form of the number is grammatically consistent. Understand the rebuttal about masculine/feminine noun pairs.
5
Examine the three positive Hanafi proofs
The text gives three arguments: (1) the number three and the plural can only apply to complete menstrual cycles, (2) the analogy with months for menopausal women shows menstruation is the basis, (3) the hadith of 'Ali on slave women. Understand each proof's logical force.

وعدة الحرة للطلاق أو الفسخ ثلاثة أقراء أي حيض [إن كانت من ذوات الحيض، وكان الفرقة بعد الدخول بها. (ع)] لما كانت الأقراء مشتركا لفظياً بين الحيض والطهر، والمراد الأول فسره بقوله: "أي حيض ولم يقل ابتداء: "ثلاث حيض" اقتداء بالنص، فعندنا الأقراء الحيض، وبه قال أحمد في الأصح، وعند الشافعي ومالك الأطهار، وبه كان يقول ابن حنبل ثم رجع لهم حديث ابن عمر ما أنه وهو عليه أمره أن يراجعها ثم ليتركها حتى تطهر ثم ليطلقها ثم قال: "فتلك العدة التي أمر الله أن تطلق لها النساء" فهذا نص على أن العدة هي الطهر ؛ ولأن تذكير الثلاثة بإثبات التاء دليل إرادة الطهر ؛ إذ لو كان المراد الحيض لقيل: "ثلاث قروء" بلا تاء؛ لأن مفرده مؤنث وهو الحيضة، ورجح علماؤنا تفسير القروء بالحيض بثلاثة وجوه أحدها: أن الله تعالى نص على الثلاثة وعلى الجمع بقوله: ﴿وَثَلَاثَةَ قُرُوءٍ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٢٨)، والثلاثة اسم لعدد معلوم لا يجوز إطلاقه على الأكثر أو الأقل، وحمله على الأطهار يؤدي إلى أنه أطلق على الأقل، وهو طهران وبعض الثالث كما هو مذهبهم وكذا الجمع الكامل هو الثلاثة، وهو حقيقة فيه، فكان أولى. ولا يقال: يجوز إطلاق لفظ الجمع على اسمين وبعض الثالثة كقوله تعالى: ﴿الْحَجُّ أَشْهُرٌ مَعْلُومَاتٌ﴾ (البقرة: ١٩٧)؛ لأنا نقول: ذلك في الجمع المجرد عن العدد، وأما العدد والجمع المقرون به فلا، الثاني: أن الله تعالى جعل الاعتداد بالشهر الاعتداد بالأقراء بقوله: ﴿وَاللَّائِي يَئِسْنَ مِنَ الْمَحِيضِ مِنْ نِسَائِكُمْ إِنِ ارْتَبْتُمْ فَعِدَّتُهُنَّ ثَلَاثَةُ أَشْهُرِ﴾ (الطلاق: ٤)، فجعل الأشهر عند عدم الحيض، فدل على أنه الأصل، والثالث: قوله علي: "طلاق الأمة اثنان وعدتها حيضتان"، فدل على أن عدة الحر ثلاث حيض، والحديث رواه أبوداود من حديث عائشة مرفوعاً، والأمة لا تتخالف الحرة في جنس ما تقع به العدة، وإنما تخالفها في العدد، وروي ذلك نصاً عن الخلفاء الراشدين والعبادلة الثلاثة وأبي بن كعب ومعاذ بن جبل وأبي الدرداء وغيرهم، ولا تمسك لهم بتذكير الثلاث؛ لأن لفظ القرء مذكر فباعتباره يذكر؛ لان الشيء إذا كان له اسمان مذكر ومؤنث كالبرد والحنطة جاز تذكيره وتأنيثه.
And the 'iddah of a free woman for divorce or annulment is three quru', i.e., menstruations, if she is of those who menstruate and the separation was after consummation. Since aqra' is ambiguous between menstruation and purity, and the intended meaning is the first, he interpreted it by saying 'i.e., menstruation' and did not say at the beginning 'three menstruations' following the text. According to us, aqra' means menstruation, and Ahmad said the same in the soundest view. According to al-Shafi'i and Malik, they are purities, and Ibn Hanbal used to say that but then retracted. Their evidence is the hadith of Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet ordered him to take her back, then leave her until she is pure, then divorce her, then said: 'That is the 'iddah which Allah commanded that women be divorced for.' This is a clear text that 'iddah is purity. And because the masculine form of the number three with the taa' indicates purity, for if the intended were menstruation, it would have been said 'thalathat quru'' without taa', because its singular is feminine, which is hiydah. Our scholars preferred the interpretation of quru' as menstruation by three aspects: first, that Allah specified three and the plural in His saying 'and three quru', and the number three is a known number that cannot be applied to more or less, and carrying it on purities leads to its application to less (two purities and part of a third) as in their school, and the complete plural is three, and it is real in that, so it is more appropriate. It cannot be said: it is permissible to apply the plural to two and part of a third, as in the verse 'the Hajj is months known' because we say: that is in a plural free of number, but with number it is not. Second, Allah made the period by months analogous to the period by quru' in His saying 'and those who have despaired of menstruation... their 'iddah is three months', making months the substitute when there is no menstruation, indicating that menstruation is the origin. Third, 'Ali's saying: 'the divorce of a slave woman is two, and her 'iddah is two menstruations', indicating that the 'iddah of a free woman is three menstruations. This hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud from 'A'isha marfu'an. A slave woman does not differ from a free woman in the type of thing that constitutes 'iddah, only in number. This is also narrated explicitly from the Rightly Guided Caliphs, the three 'Abdullahs, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Abu al-Darda', and others. Their argument from the masculine form of three does not hold, because the word qur' is masculine, so it is made masculine on its basis; when a thing has two names, one masculine and one feminine like al-bard (cold) and al-hintah (wheat), it can be treated as masculine or feminine.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a detailed inter-madhhab dispute over the interpretation of aqra' in the verse 'and divorced women shall wait for three quru''. The Hanafi school holds that quru' means menstruations (hayd), while the Shafi'i and Maliki schools hold it means purities (tuhr). The commentator first notes that aqra' is ambiguous, and the author interprets it as hayd. He then gives the Shafi'i evidence: the hadith of Ibn 'Umar where the Prophet said that 'iddah is the purity (tuhr) in which divorce should be given, and the grammatical argument that the number three with a feminine suffix indicates tuhr. The Hanafi response refutes these: (1) The verse 'three quru' specifies the number three and the complete plural, so it cannot refer to two purities and part of a third. (2) The verse about those who have despaired of menstruation makes three months the substitute, showing that menstruation is the basis. (3) The hadith about slave women 'iddah being two menstruations implies that a free woman's is three, as the type is the same; only the number differs. The Hanafis also cite numerous companion narrations. Finally, they argue that the grammatical point is invalid because qur' can be masculine or feminine.

Key Terms
القرء (al-qur'): Plural aqra'/quru'. Ambiguous term meaning either menstruation or purity. Here, the Hanafi school defines it as menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what does 'three quru'' mean for a free woman's 'iddah?
A) Three menstrual cycles
B) Three clean periods
C) Three months
D) Three weeks

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'quru'' in the 'iddah verse as menstruations, arguing from the numerical definitiveness of 'three', the analogy with months, and the hadith of 'Ali, and this definition is the relied-upon view for fatwa.

'Iddah for Non-Menstruating Women: Three Months with an Exception
Explains that women who do not menstruate observe a three-month 'iddah, with an exception for adult virgins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah for non-menstruating women according to the Hanafi school, explain the Quranic proof, and evaluate the exception for the adult virgin based on the later fatwa.

Core Idea
Non-menstruating women have a three-month 'iddah by Quranic text, but later necessity requires a one-year 'iddah for adult virgins.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies the 'iddah for women who do not menstruate—whether due to young age or menopause. It presents the primary Quranic ruling from Surat al-Talaq (65:4) and introduces a later pragmatic shift in the Hanafi fatwa for the adult virgin (al-anisah). By examining both the textual proof and the subsequent adaptation, we see how fiqh interacts with changing social conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of non-menstruating women
The passage mentions women who do not menstruate due to young age, menopause, or reaching age fifteen without menses. These are distinct from women who menstruate but have prolonged purity.
Three categories are implied: pre-pubescent (saghira), menopausal (yaisah), and those who never menstruated though of age (anisa).
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse (65:4) is cited for both 'those who have despaired of menstruation' and 'those who have not menstruated'. Identify the exact wording and reference.
The verse explicitly prescribes three months for both groups.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit mention of 'three months' in the verse directly indicates the period. The imperative or declarative form establishes the obligation.
The text treats the verse as clear (nass) on the period.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with Imam Malik
Imam Malik holds a one-year 'iddah (or nine months) for the adult virgin, based on precaution (ihtiyat) and the need for istibra' (clearance of the womb).
This difference is reported in the passage.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih and later change
The original Hanafi ruling is three months, but later Hanafi scholars adopted Malik's view for the anisa due to necessity (darura). The passage states that 'the fatwa in our time is on Malik's view'.
This shows how fatwa can adapt while preserving the original textual ruling for other categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ثلاثة أشهر إن لم تحض: لصغر أو إياس أو بلوغ بالسن أي خمسة عشرة سنة من غير رؤية حيض؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّائِي يَئِسْنَ مِنَ الْمَحِيضِ﴾ (الطلاق:٤)، قيد به؛ لأن التي حاضت امتد طهرها فلا تعتد بالأشهر إلا إذا بلغت سن الإياس، وعن مالك: انقضاؤها بحول، وقيل بتسعة أشهر : ستة لاستبراء الرحم وثلاثة أشهر للعدة والفتوى في زماننا على قول مالك في عدة الآنسة للضرورة، أما الصغائر التي لم يحضن فعدتهن ثلاثة أشهر؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّائِي لَمْ يَحِضْنَ﴾ (الطلاق:٤)
Or three months if she does not menstruate: due to young age, menopause, or reaching the age of fifteen years without seeing menstruation; based on His statement: 'and those who have despaired of menstruation from among your women, if you are in doubt, their 'iddah is three months' (65:4). It is restricted by this because a woman who menstruates but whose tahar stretches does not count by months unless she reaches menopause. From Malik: it ends after a year; it was said nine months: six for istibra' of the womb and three for 'iddah. The fatwa in our time is on Malik's view regarding the 'iddah of a young unmarried virgin due to necessity. As for young girls who have not menstruated, their 'iddah is three months due to His statement: 'and those who have not menstruated' (65:4).
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for women who do not menstruate, either because they are pre-pubescent or menopausal. The proof is from Surat al-Talaq (65:4) which mentions both categories. The commentator notes that a woman who menstruates but later experiences prolonged purity (istimrar al-tuhr) does not switch to months unless she reaches the age of menopause (usually fifty-five or older; defined here as fifteen years without menses?). The text also mentions Malik's opinion that a young unmarried virgin (anisa) should wait a full year (six months for istibra' and three for 'iddah? Actually the text says 'a year' then 'nine months' but seems contradictory. It says 'al-fatwa fi zamanina' follows Malik's view for the anisa due to necessity. However, the clear ruling of the Hanafi school for those who have never menstruated (sagha'ir) is three months based on the Quran.

Key Terms
الآنسة (al-anisah): A young unmarried virgin who has not reached menopause.

The Widow's 'Iddah: Four Months Ten Days
Ruling on widow's 'iddah with four disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah for a widow according to the Hanafi school, identify its Quranic proof, and explain the Hanafi positions on the four disputed issues regarding its computation.

Core Idea
The widow's 'iddah is four months and ten days from death, based on Quran 2:234, abrogating earlier practice.

This passage establishes the waiting period for a widow — four months and ten days — based on clear Quranic text. It then addresses four key disagreements among the salaf on how this 'iddah is counted, which the Hanafi school resolves with specific arguments. Understanding these disputes clarifies the school's method of dealing with apparent conflicts between Quranic verses and differing companion opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its primary proof
Locate the ruling: a widow observes 'iddah for four months and ten days. Note the Quranic verse cited: 2:234.
The ruling applies to all free women, Muslim or dhimmi, consummated or not.
2
Analyze the first dispute: two 'iddahs or one abrogated
Some salaf said a widow had two 'iddahs: a year (from 2:240) and four months ten days. The Hanafi response: 2:240 is abrogated by 2:234.
The longer 'iddah was pre-Islamic custom.
3
Analyze the second dispute: counting ten days and nights
Hanafi count includes ten days and ten nights; 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr counted nine nights and ten days. The Hanafi proof: mentioning 'ten' with 'days' implies both days and nights.
Practical effect: she can marry after ten full days and nights, i.e., on the eleventh day.
4
Examine the third dispute: pregnant widow
Hanafi view: she waits until delivery; 'Ali said she waits the longer of delivery or four months ten days. Hanafi follows 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud.
If delivery occurs before four months, she may remarry immediately after.
5
Examine the fourth dispute: start time and applicability to dhimmi
Hanafi: 'iddah starts from death (not knowledge). This is from Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn 'Abbas. Also, the verse applies to dhimmi women, contra Malik who required less.
Malik only required istibra' (one menstrual cycle) for dhimmi widows if consummated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللموت أي عدة الحرة صغيرة كانت أو كبيرة، ذمية أو مسلمة، موطوءة أو لا لموت الزوج. (ط،ع) أربعة أشهر وعشر أي عدة الوفاة أربعة أشهر وعشر؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنْكُمْ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٣٤)، واختلف السلف في هذه المسألة في فصول أربعة: الأول: أن من السلف من يقول لها عدتان: الطولى، وهي الحول، والقصرى وهي أربعة أشهر وعشر؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَصِيَّةً لِأَزْوَاجِهِمْ مَتَاعاً إِلَى الْحَوْلِ غَيْرَ إِخْرَاجِ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٤٠) أي بعد أربعة أشهر فَلا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْكُمْ ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٣٣)، والجواب: أن هذه الآية منسوخة لقوله تعالى: ﴿يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنْفُسِهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْراً﴾ (البقرة: ٢٣٤). الثاني: أن المعتبر عشرة أيام وعشر ليال من الشهر الخامس عندنا، وكان عبد الله بن عمرو بن العاص ما يقول عشر ليال وتسعة أيام، حتى يجوز لها أن تتزوج في اليوم العاشر؛ لظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَشْراً﴾. والجواب: أن ذكر أحد العددين من الأيام والليالي بعبارة الجمع يقتضي دخول ما بإزائه من العدد الآخر. الثالث: أن المتوفى عنها زوجها لو كانت حاملاً تعتد بوضع الحمل عندنا، وهو قول عمر وابن مسعود ها، وكان علي الله يقول : تعتد بأبعد الأجلين، إما بوضع الحمل أو بأربعة أشهر وعشر الرابع أن عدة الوفاة معتبرة من وقت موت الزوج عندنا، وهو قول ابن مسعود وابن عباس ما ، وكان عليه الله يقول : من حين تعلم. والآية التي تلونا حجة على مالك في الكتابية حيث أوجب الاستبراء عليها فقط إن كانت مدخولاً بها، ولم يوجب شيئاً على غير المدخول بها.
And for death: i.e., the 'iddah of a free woman, whether young or old, dhimmi or Muslim, whether consummated or not, due to the death of the husband. Four months and ten: i.e., the 'iddah of death is four months and ten, based on His statement: 'And those who are taken by death among you' (2:234). The salaf differed on this issue in four points: First: Some of the salaf say she has two 'iddahs: the longer, which is a year, and the shorter, which is four months and ten, based on His statement: 'a will for their wives, provision for a year without expulsion' (2:240) meaning after four months 'then no sin upon you' (2:233). The answer: this verse is abrogated by His statement: 'they shall wait for themselves four months and ten' (2:234). Second: The consideration is ten days and ten nights of the fifth month according to us. 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr ibn al-'As used to say ten nights and nine days, so that she may marry on the tenth day, due to the apparent meaning of 'and ten'. The answer: mentioning one of the two numbers (days or nights) in the phrase 'and ten' implies the entry of the corresponding number. Third: The widow if pregnant waits until delivery according to us, and this is the view of 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. 'Ali used to say she waits for the longer of the two periods, either delivery or four months and ten. Fourth: The 'iddah of death is counted from the time of the husband's death according to us, and this is the view of Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn 'Abbas. 'Ali used to say from the time she learns. The verse we recited is a proof against Malik regarding a dhimmi woman, where he obligates istibra' only if she was consummated, and does not obligate anything on the unconsummated.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage gives the 'iddah for a widow: four months and ten days, based on the Quran (2:234). It then addresses four disputes among the salaf. (1) The pre-Islamic practice of a one-year 'iddah derived from 2:240 is abrogated by 2:234. (2) The counting of ten days: the Hanafi view is ten days and ten nights (so 10 full days), while 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr held ten nights and nine days. The Hanafi argument: mentioning 'ten' with the plural 'days' implies both days and nights are counted. (3) A pregnant widow waits until delivery; if it takes longer than four months ten days, she waits until delivery; if less, she waits the full period? Actually the Hanafi view is that she waits only until delivery; 'Ali said she waits the longer of the two. Our school follows 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. (4) The start of the 'iddah: from the moment of death, not from when she learns of it. The final note: The verse requiring 'iddah for widows applies to Muslim and dhimmi women alike, refuting Malik who only required istibra' for a dhimmi woman if consummated, and nothing if not.

Key Terms
عدة الوفاة ('iddat al-wafah): The waiting period for a widow: four months and ten days from the husband's death.
ناسخ ومنسوخ (nasikh wa mansukh): Abrogation: the later verse (2:234) abrogates the earlier (2:240).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) At the moment of the husband's death
C) After the funeral
D) When she receives the inheritance

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school firmly holds that the widow's 'iddah is four months and ten days from the moment of death, with specific resolutions for subsidiary disputes based on textual evidence and companion practice.

'Iddah of a Slave Woman: Two Quru'
States the 'iddah of a slave woman as two menstrual cycles with evidence and reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah of a slave woman for divorce, explain the evidence and reasoning, and identify the conditions for its application.

Core Idea
A slave woman's 'iddah is two menstrual cycles because slavery halves the free woman's three cycles, and a cycle cannot be fractioned.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the 'iddah of a slave woman. While a free woman observes three quru' after divorce, a slave woman's 'iddah is reduced to two due to her status. The ruling is grounded in a specific hadith and the principle of halving, acknowledging that a menstrual cycle cannot be split. We will explore the evidence, the reasoning, and the scope of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What exactly is the 'iddah for a slave woman? The passage states it is 'two quru'.' Understand that quru' here means menstrual cycles.
The term quru' is used for both clean periods and menstruation; Hanafis interpret it as menstruation in 'iddah.
2
Locate the evidence
The passage cites a hadith: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two pronouncements and her 'iddah is two menstruations.' Recognise this as direct textual proof.
3
Analyse the reasoning
Why two and not one? Because slavery reduces the 'iddah to half of a free woman's three cycles. Half of three is 1.5, but a menstrual cycle cannot be divided, so it becomes two complete cycles. Umar's statement about wishing for one and a half shows the limit of halving.
The principle is takmil: when halving yields a fraction, the number is completed to the next whole.
4
Note the scope
The ruling applies to all types of female slaves after consummation, as long as they menstruate: ordinary slaves, mudabbara, mukataba, umm walad, and partially freed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمة قرءان: أي عدة الأمة في الطلاق والفسخ حيضتان إن كانت ممن تحيض؛ لقوله عليه: "طلاق الأمة تطليقتان وعدتها حيضتان"، ولأن الرق متنصف والحيضة لا تتجزأ فكملت حيضتان، قال عمر ه: لو استطعت لجعلتها حيضة ونصفاً، وهذا في الطلاق بعد الدخول لمن تحيض سواء كانت أمة أو مدبرة أو مكاتبة أو أم ولد أو معتقة البعض.
And for a slave woman: two quru', i.e., the 'iddah of a slave woman in divorce and annulment is two menstruations if she is among those who menstruate; based on his (peace be upon him) saying: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two pronouncements and her 'iddah is two menstruations.' And because slavery reduces by half, and menstruation cannot be divided, so two complete menstruations. 'Umar said: 'If I could, I would have made it one and a half.' This applies to divorce after consummation for those who menstruate, whether she is a slave, mudabbara, mukataba, umm walad, or partially freed.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage gives the 'iddah for a slave woman: two quru' (i.e., two menstrual cycles). The proof is a hadith: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two pronouncements and her 'iddah is two menstruations.' The reasoning is that because of slavery, the 'iddah is reduced to half of a free woman's three. Since a full menstrual cycle cannot be halved, it becomes two complete cycles. 'Umar wished he could make it one and a half, but that's not possible. The ruling applies to all categories of slaves (absolute, mudabbara, mukataba, umm walad, and partially free) after consummation, as long as they menstruate.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-ammah): A female slave. Here, any female in a state of slavery for 'iddah purposes.

Iddah of a Free Woman: Three Menstrual Cycles or Three Months
Iddah rulings with linguistic and Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the iddah periods for a free woman and a slave woman after talaq, and explain the Quranic evidence and the Hanafi interpretation of quru'.

Core Idea
The iddah of a free woman after divorce is three menstrual cycles or three months if she does not menstruate.

This lesson covers the Hanafi ruling on the waiting period (iddah) for women after divorce. The Quran provides guidance for menstruating and non-menstruating women, and the Hanafi school interprets the key term 'quru'' as menstrual cycles. We will examine the evidence, including verses from Surah At-Talaq and the consensus of the Companions, and also touch on a difference with Imam Malik's school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core iddah rulings
State the three main rulings from the matn: free woman after talaq (three cycles or three months), death (4 months 10 days), and slave woman (two cycles).
Focus on the free woman's iddah for this lesson.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the Quranic evidence cited for the three-month iddah: 'And those who despair of menstruation...' (At-Talaq:4).
The verse is used for women in menopause and those who have not menstruated.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the imperative and the specified time frame indicate the obligation of a three-month iddah for non-menstruating women.
The Hanafi school uses the zahir (apparent meaning) to derive the ruling.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the Maliki position that a non-menstruating woman waits a full year, and mention the fatwa in our time following Malik for the maiden.
This ikhtilaf is acknowledged but the Hanafi position remains the relied-upon view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Determine why the Hanafi position is preferred: stronger Quranic evidence and consensus of the Companions.
The text explicitly lists the Rightly Guided Caliphs and several Companions as supporters.

Chapter of Iddah: Or three months if she does not menstruate, and for death four months and ten days, and the slave woman two menstrual cycles. = Meaning the iddah of a free woman due to young age or old age. In the type of thing by which iddah occurs, and it differs from it in number. This has been related as text from the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the three Abdallahs and Ubayy ibn Ka'b and Mu'adh ibn Jabal and Abu Darda and others. And they have no proof from the masculine form of three, because the word quru' is masculine, so by considering it, it is masculine; because if a thing has two names, masculine and feminine, like 'cold' and 'wheat', it is permissible to use it masculine and feminine. (Fath, 'Ayni) If she does not menstruate: due to young age or menopause or reaching the age of fifteen years without seeing menstruation; because of Allah's saying: 'And those who despair of menstruation' (At-Talaq:4), it is restricted because if she menstruated, her purity period lengthened, so she does not observe iddah by months unless she reaches the age of menopause. Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on iddah (waiting period). The matn states three rulings: the iddah of a free woman after talaq is three menstrual cycles; if she does not menstruate, three months; the iddah of death is four months and ten days; and the iddah of a slave woman is two menstrual cycles. The commentary begins by explaining that the word 'quru' refers to menstrual cycles, and the evidence comes from the Quran and consensus of the Companions. It then addresses the objection that the phrase 'three quru' is masculine, whereas the well-known usage is feminine; the response is that the word can be used in both genders. Next, it discusses the case of a woman who does not menstruate due to youth or menopause, citing the verse 'And those who despair of menstruation...' and 'And those who have not menstruated...' to establish three months. It also mentions Imam Malik's opinion that such a woman waits a full year, but for necessity, the fatwa follows Malik in the case of a mature virgin who has not menstruated. The passage focuses on the free woman's iddah and the reasoning behind it.

Key Terms
قرء (quru'): In this text, it means menstrual cycle (hayd), not purity period.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.
إياس (iyas): Menopause, the age at which a woman no longer menstruates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the iddah of a slave woman according to the Hanafi school?
A) One menstrual cycle
B) Two menstrual cycles
C) Three menstrual cycles
D) Three months

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines quru' as menstrual cycles and rules that the iddah of a free woman after talaq is three cycles, or three months if she does not menstruate, based on the Quran and consensus of the Companions.

Iddah of Death: Four Months and Ten Days
Explanation of the iddah of a widow: four months and ten days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the iddah of death and explain how four pre-Islamic disputes are resolved using Quranic evidence and naskh.

Core Idea
The iddah of death is four months and ten days for all widows, based on a clear Quranic text that abrogates earlier provisions.

This lesson examines the waiting period for a widow after her husband's death. You will learn the primary evidence from Surah Al-Baqarah, how the Hanafi school resolves apparent conflicts with other verses, and the reasoning behind several subsidiary issues regarding the count and start of the iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Determine the Hanafi position: the iddah of death is four months and ten days. Note that this applies to all free women, regardless of age, religion, or whether the marriage was consummated.
The ruling is wajib based on the Quran.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the evidence from Quran 2:234: 'yatarabbasna bi anfusihinna arba'ata ashurin wa ashran.' The imperative indicates obligation.
The dalil is qat'i in thubut and dalala.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the first dispute
Some Salaf claimed two iddahs (a year and four months ten) based on Quran 2:240. The Hanafi response is that verse 2:240 is abrogated (naskh) by 2:234.
Naskh is accepted when a later verse supersedes an earlier one.
4
Examine the remaining three disputes
Second: the count of 'ten' includes both days and nights. Third: a pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery, not the longer of two terms. Fourth: the iddah begins from the time of death, not from when she learns of it.
Hanafi positions are supported by Quranic usage and companion statements.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The established Hanafi ruling is that the iddah of death is four months and ten days for all free widows, and for a pregnant woman it ends at delivery.
This is the position in Zahir al-Riwayah and used for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللموت أي عدة الحرة صغيرة كانت أو كبيرة، ذمية أو مسلمة، موطوءة أو لا لموت الزوج. (ط،ع) أربعة أشهر وعشر أي عدة الوفاة أربعة أشهر وعشر؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنْكُمْ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٣٤)، واختلف السلف في هذه المسألة في فصول أربعة: الأول: أن من السلف من يقول لها عدتان: الطولى، وهي الحول، والقصرى وهي أربعة أشهر وعشر؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَصِيَّةً لِأَزْوَاجِهِمْ مَتَاعاً إِلَى الْحَوْلِ غَيْرَ إِخْرَاجِ فَإِنْ خَرَجْنَ ﴾ (البقرة : ٢٤٠) أي بعد أربعة أشهر فَلا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْكُمْ ﴾ (البقرة : ۲۳۳)، والجواب: أن هذه الآية منسوخة لقوله تعالى: يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنْفُسِهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْراً (البقرة: ٢٣٤). الثاني: أن المعتبر عشرة أيام وعشر ليال من الشهر الخامس عندنا، وكان عبد الله بن عمرو بن العاص ما يقول عشر ليال وتسعة أيام، حتى يجوز لها أن تتزوج في اليوم العاشر؛ لظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَشْراً.
والجواب: أن ذكر أحد العددين من الأيام والليالي بعبارة الجمع يقتضي دخول ما بإزائه من العدد الآخر. الثالث: أن المتوفى عنها زوجها لو كانت حاملاً تعتد بوضع الحمل عندنا، وهو قول عمر وابن مسعود ها، وكان علي الله يقول : تعتد بأبعد الأجلين، إما بوضع الحمل أو بأربعة أشهر وعشر الرابع أن عدة الوفاة معتبرة من وقت موت الزوج عندنا، وهو قول ابن مسعود وابن عباس ما ، وكان عليه الله يقول : من حين تعلم. والآية التي تلونا حجة على مالك في الكتابية حيث أوجب الاستبراء عليها فقط إن كانت مدخولاً بها، ولم يوجب شيئاً على غير المدخول بها. (فتح عيني)
And for death: meaning the iddah of a free woman whether small or large, dhimmiya or Muslim, whether intercourse occurred or not, due to the husband's death. (T, 'A) Four months and ten: meaning the iddah of death is four months and ten; because of Allah's saying: 'And those who are taken from you' (Al-Baqarah:234). The Salaf differed on this matter in four points: First: Some of the Salaf say she has two iddahs: the longer which is a year, and the shorter which is four months and ten; due to Allah's saying: 'A will for their wives provision until the year without expulsion' (Al-Baqarah:240) meaning after four months 'then no sin upon you' (Al-Baqarah:233). The answer: This verse is abrogated by Allah's saying: 'They shall wait with themselves four months and ten' (Al-Baqarah:234). Second: The consideration at our school is ten days and ten nights from the fifth month, and Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As used to say ten nights and nine days, so she may marry on the tenth day, due to the apparent meaning of His saying: 'and ten.' The answer: Mentioning one of the two numbers of days and nights by a collective expression implies the inclusion of the corresponding number. Third: If the woman whose husband died was pregnant, she observes iddah by delivery according to us, which is the statement of Umar and Ibn Mas'ud, but Ali used to say: She observes the longer of the two terms, either delivery or four months and ten. Fourth: The iddah of death is considered from the time of the husband's death according to us, which is the statement of Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Abbas, but he (Ali) used to say: From the time she knows. And the verse we recited is a proof against Malik regarding the dhimmiya where he required istibra' only if she was entered upon, and did not require anything for the non-entered upon. (Fath 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage explains the iddah of a widow. It begins by clarifying that this ruling applies to all free women regardless of age, religion, or consummation. The primary evidence is the Quranic verse (2:234). The commentary then presents four disputes among the Salaf: (1) Some held she has two iddahs, a year (based on 2:240) and four months ten (2:234); the response is that 2:240 is abrogated. (2) The count of ten days from the fifth month: the Hanafi position is ten days and ten nights, while Abdullah ibn Amr said ten nights and nine days; the response is that mentioning 'ashran (ten) implies both days and nights. (3) For a pregnant widow, the Hanafi view is that delivery ends the iddah (later discussed in detail), while Ali said the longer of the two terms. (4) The iddah starts from the time of death, not from when the woman learns of it. The passage also notes that the verse is evidence against Malik's view that a non-Muslim widow need only observe istibra' (one menstrual cycle) if entered upon. The Hanafi position is that the full iddah of death applies to all widows, including dhimmiyas.

Key Terms
عدة الوفاة (iddat al-wafat): The waiting period for a widow: four months and ten days.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of an earlier revealed ruling by a later one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many days are included in the 'ten' of the iddah of death?
A) Ten days only
B) Ten nights only
C) Ten days and ten nights
D) Ten days and nine nights

Key Takeaway
The iddah of death for a widow is four months and ten days, based on a clear Quranic command that abrogates earlier provisions, and applies to all free women regardless of religion or consummation.

Iddah of Slave Women
Explanation of iddah for slave women: two cycles or half of prescribed period.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the iddah of a slave woman in divorce and death, and explain the qiyas basis for halving the free woman's iddah.

Core Idea
Slave iddah is half of free iddah because slavery halves rights; for menstruating women: two cycles; for non-menstruating or death: half the months.

This lesson explains the iddah for female slaves, which is half that of free women. The ruling is derived from a hadith and supported by qiyas based on the principle that slavery halves legal rights. The specific durations apply to both divorce and death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the free iddah
Recall that a free woman's iddah is three menstrual cycles (or three months if not menstruating) for divorce, and four months and ten days for death.
2
Learn the hadith for slave iddah
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two divorces and her iddah is two menstruations.' This is a specific proof.
This hadith establishes the rule for menstruating slave women.
3
Apply the qiyas principle
Since slavery halves rights (e.g., punishment, marriage capacity), the iddah is halved. However, a menstruation cannot be halved, so it becomes two cycles instead of one and a half.
The principle is 'al-raq tanassaf' (slavery halves).
4
Determine iddah for non-menstruating or death
For a slave woman who does not menstruate due to youth or old age, the iddah is one and a half months in divorce and two months and five days in death.
Months can be divided, so half is applied directly.
5
Note the consensus and inclusivity
The companions agreed to these rulings, and all types of slaves (qinn, mudabbira, etc.) fall under this rule.

Source Text
