والأمة قرءان: أي عدة الأمة في الطلاق والفسخ حيضتان إن كانت ممن تحيض؛ لقوله عليه: "طلاق الأمة تطليقتان وعدتها حيضتان ، ولأن الرق ،متنصف والحيضة لا تتجزأ فكملت ،حيضتان، قال عمر ه: لو استطعت لجعلتها حيضة ونصفاً، وهذا في الطلاق بعد الدخول لمن تحض سواء كانت أمة أو مدبرة أو مكاتبة أو أم ولد أو معتقة البعض. (فتح، عيني)

كتاب الطلاق
٥٠٢
ونصف المقدّر، والحامل وضعه وزوجة الفارّ أبعد الأجلين ومن عتقت
أي وضع الحمل
باب العدة
من عدة الطلاق والوفاة أي عدة الأمة إذا عتقت
ونصف المقدر أي إن كانت ممن لا تحيض لصغر أو كبر أو كانت متوفى عنها زوجها فنصف ما قدر فيه الحرة، وهو شهر ونصف في الطلاق بعد الدخول وشهران وخمس أيام في الوفاة، وعليه إجماع الأمة. [تبيين الحقائق: ٢٥٢/٣] لأن الشهر يتجزأ والرق يتنصف ولا فرق في ذلك في القنة وغيرها عند أبي حنيفة؛ لوجود الرق في الكل. (مستخلص، عيني)
والحامل وضعه [ أي عدة الحامل سواء كانت حرة أو أمة أو مطلقة أو متوفى عنها زوجها. (ط)] أي عدة الحامل أن تضع حملها سواء كانت في عدة الطلاق أو الفسخ أو في عدة الوفاة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُولاتُ الْأَحْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَنْ يَضَعْنَ حَمْلَهُنَّ (الطلاق:٤)، هو قول ابن مسعود وعمر ما، وقال علي الله: عدتها أبعد الأجلين؛ لأن النصوص متعارضة، بعضها يوجب تربص ثلاثة قروء كما في سورة البقرة، وبعضها أربعة أشهر وعشراً كما فيها أيضاً، وبعضها وضع الحمل كما في سورة الطلاق، فقلنا: بوجوب الأبعد احتياطاً، والجواب: أن آية الحمل متأخرة فيكون غيرها منسوخاً بها أو مخصوصاً، قال ابن مسعود الله: "من شاء باهلته أن سورة النساء القصرى نزلت بعد أربعة أشهر وعشر"، رواه أبوداود والنسائي وابن ماجه. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٩٦/١] يريد أن قوله: ﴿وَأُولاتُ الْأَحْمَالِ ) متأخر عن قوله: يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنْفُسِهِنَّ ﴾ (البقرة:۲۲۸)، فيكون ناسخاً في ذوات الأحمال. (مستخلص، فتح) وزوجة الفار أبعد الأجلين: أي عدة زوجة المريض الذي مات في مرضه وطلقها بائناً أو ثلاثاً فعدتها أبعد الأجلين من عدة الوفاة ومن عدة الطلاق، يعني إن كانت تحيض ثلاث حيض في أقل من أربعة أشهر وعشر اعتدت بأربعة أشهر وعشر؛ لأنها أطول من ثلاث حيض، وإن كانت تحيض ثلاث حيض في أكثر ذلك من اعتدت بالحيض، وهذا عند الطرفين، وعند أبي يوسف: تعتد عدة الطلاق وهو القياس؛ لأن النكاح زال بالطلاق وبقي في حق الإرث حكماً احتياطاً؛ لإجماع الصحابة، وجه الاستحسان على قولهما: أنها لما ورثت جعل قائماً حكماً إلى الوفاة؛ إذ لا إرث إلا به، فكذا في حق العدة، بل أولى، أما إذا كان الطلاق رجعياً فعليها عدة الوفاة إجماعاً [ رمز الحقائق ٢٩٦/١](مستخلص، فتح)
النكاح
انتقلت
عتقت إلخ: يعني إذا طلق امرأته الأمة طلاقاً رجعياً، ثم أعتقها المولى في العدة ومن من الطلاق الرجعي عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر؛ لأن النكاح باق من كل وجه، لا فيما إذا عتقت في عدة الطلاق البائن، ولا في عدة موت الزوج؛ لزوال النكاح ولم يتكامل الملك بعده والطلاق في الملك الناقص لا يوجب عدة الحرائر، فلا تنتقل عدتها إليها، بخلاف ما لو آلى منها ثم أبانها ثم أعتقها سيدها في العدة، حيث تصير مدة إيلائها مدة إيلاء الحرائر، ولا فرق فيه بين البائن والرجعي؛ لأن البينونة ليست من أحكام الإيلاء، فالبائن والرجعي فيه سواء بخلاف العدة، فإن سببها الطلاق وهي تعقبه فيعتبر فيها صفته، وقال مالك له: لا تزاد بالعتق، أي عدتها حيضتان كما كانت، وقال الشافعي له: عدتها عدة الحرائر في البائن والموت أيضاً. (عيني، مسكين)
And the slave woman: two menstrual cycles: meaning the iddah of a slave woman in divorce and annulment is two menstruations if she menstruates; because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two divorces and her iddah is two menstruations', and because slavery is half (of freedom) and a menstruation cannot be divided, so two complete menstruations. Umar said: If I could, I would have made it one and a half menstruations. This applies to divorce after consummation for those who menstruate, whether she is a concubine, mudabbira, maktuba, umm walad, or partially freed. (Fath, 'Ayni) 

And half of the prescribed period: meaning if she is one who does not menstruate due to youth or old age or if her husband died, then half of what is prescribed for a free woman, which is one and a half months in divorce after consummation and two months and five days in death, and upon this is the consensus of the Ummah. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 3/252] Because a month can be divided and slavery is half, and there is no difference in this between a qinn and others according to Abu Hanifa, because slavery exists in all. (Mustakhlas, 'Ayni) 

And the pregnant woman: her delivery [meaning the iddah of a pregnant woman, whether free or slave, divorced or widowed. (T)] meaning the iddah of a pregnant woman is to deliver her pregnancy whether she is in iddah of divorce, annulment, or death; because of Allah's saying: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their pregnancy' (At-Talaq:4). This is the saying of Ibn Mas'ud and Umar, and Ali said: Her iddah is the longer of the two terms; because the texts conflict, some requiring three menstrual periods as in Surat al-Baqarah, some four months and ten as in it, and some delivery as in Surat at-Talaq, so we said: the longer is required as a precaution. The answer: The verse of pregnancy is later, so others are abrogated or specified by it. Ibn Mas'ud said: 'Whoever wishes, I will debate him that the shorter Surah an-Nisa was revealed after four months and ten,' reported by Abu Dawood, an-Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/296] He meant that His saying 'And those who are pregnant' came after His saying 'They shall wait with themselves' (Al-Baqarah:228), so it is abrogating for pregnant women. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 

And the wife of the farer (the man who fled): the longer of the two terms: meaning the iddah of the wife of a sick man who died in his illness and divorced her irrevocably or three times, her iddah is the longer of the two terms from the iddah of death and the iddah of divorce. That is, if she menstruates three cycles in less than four months and ten, she observes four months and ten because it is longer than three cycles; if she menstruates three cycles in more than that, she observes iddah by menstruation. This is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf, she observes the iddah of divorce, which is the analogy, because the marriage ended by divorce and remains in effect for inheritance only as a judicial precaution due to the consensus of the Companions. The istihsan reasoning according to them is that since she inherits, the marriage is considered effective until death, because there is no inheritance without it, so likewise for iddah, even more. If the divorce was revocable, she observes the iddah of death by consensus. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/296] (Mustakhlas, Fath) 

'And whoever is freed' etc.: meaning if he divorces his slave wife with a revocable divorce, then the owner frees her during the iddah, then her iddah changes to that of free women, because the marriage remains in every respect. Not so if she is freed during the iddah of an irrevocable divorce or during the iddah of death of the husband, because the marriage has ended and complete ownership is not achieved afterwards, and divorce under incomplete ownership does not require the iddah of free women, so her iddah does not change. This is different from if he pronounced ila' on her and then divorced her irrevocably and then her master freed her during the iddah, where the period of ila' becomes the period of ila' of free women, and there is no difference in that between irrevocable and revocable, because the final separation is not part of the rulings of ila', so they are equal. Malik said: She is not increased by freedom, i.e., her iddah remains two cycles as before. Shafi'i said: Her iddah is that of free women in irrevocable divorce and death as well. ('Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings on iddah. First, for a slave woman, the iddah after divorce is two menstrual cycles based on the hadith and the principle that slavery halves the iddah. Umar's statement indicates he would have made it one and a half if possible. This applies to all types of slaves (qinn, mudabbira, etc.). Second, for a slave woman who does not menstruate or whose husband dies, the iddah is half of the prescribed period for a free woman: one and a half months for divorce, two months and five days for death. Third, for any pregnant woman (free or slave, divorced or widowed), her iddah ends with delivery, based on the Quran (65:4). The text refutes Ali's view of the longer term by stating that the pregnancy verse abrogates the earlier verses. Fourth, the case of a woman whose husband divorced her in his illness (farer) – her iddah is the longer of the death iddah and divorce iddah, because she inherits. Abu Yusuf differs, saying the iddah should be based on divorce, but the preferred view (istihsan) by the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is the longer term. Fifth, if a slave woman is freed during a revocable divorce iddah, her iddah changes to that of a free woman. If it is during an irrevocable divorce or death iddah, it does not change. This is different from the case of ila'. The passage also notes the views of Malik (no change) and Shafi'i (change in both irrevocable and death).

Key Terms
أمة (amah): A female slave

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the iddah of a slave woman who does not menstruate due to young age and is divorced after consummation?
A) Three months
B) One and a half months
C) Two months
D) Four months and ten days

Key Takeaway
The iddah of a slave woman is half that of a free woman, adjusted for indivisible periods.

Iddah of Pregnant Woman and Wife of Farer
Explanation of iddah for pregnant women (delivery) and wife of farer (longer term).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the iddah of a pregnant woman (delivery) and the iddah of the wife of a man who divorces his wife during his fatal illness (farer), and explain the Hanafi evidence for both.

Core Idea
A pregnant woman's iddah ends with delivery; a wife divorced during her husband's fatal illness observes the longer of the two iddahs to safeguard inheritance.

This lesson covers two special iddah cases: the pregnant woman, whose iddah ends upon delivery regardless of the type of marriage dissolution, and the wife of a farer (a man who divorces his wife in his terminal illness). The Hanafi school uses istihsan to give the wife the longer iddah to protect her inheritance rights.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Quranic verse for pregnant women
Allah says: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their pregnancy' (65:4). This is the primary evidence.
2
Address the apparent conflict with other verses
Verses in Surat al-Baqarah mention iddah of three months or four months ten days. The pregnancy verse is later and abrogates those for pregnant women.
Ibn Mas'ud attests to the later revelation.
3
Apply the rule to all pregnant women
Regardless of being free or slave, divorced or widowed, the iddah ends with delivery.
4
For the wife of the farer, identify the scenario
A man divorces his wife irrevocably or with three divorces while suffering from a fatal illness, and dies. The wife inherits, and her iddah is the longer of death iddah (4 months 10 days) or divorce iddah (3 cycles).
5
Understand the istihsan reasoning vs. qiyas
Abu Yusuf says iddah of divorce (qiyas), but Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the longer term (istihsan) because the marriage is considered ongoing for inheritance.
If the divorce was revocable, consensus gives death iddah.

والأمة قرءان: أي عدة الأمة في الطلاق والفسخ حيضتان إن كانت ممن تحيض؛ لقوله عليه: "طلاق الأمة تطليقتان وعدتها حيضتان ، ولأن الرق ،متنصف والحيضة لا تتجزأ فكملت ،حيضتان، قال عمر ه: لو استطعت لجعلتها حيضة ونصفاً، وهذا في الطلاق بعد الدخول لمن تحض سواء كانت أمة أو مدبرة أو مكاتبة أو أم ولد أو معتقة البعض. (فتح، عيني)

كتاب الطلاق
٥٠٢
ونصف المقدّر، والحامل وضعه وزوجة الفارّ أبعد الأجلين ومن عتقت
أي وضع الحمل
باب العدة
من عدة الطلاق والوفاة أي عدة الأمة إذا عتقت
ونصف المقدر أي إن كانت ممن لا تحيض لصغر أو كبر أو كانت متوفى عنها زوجها فنصف ما قدر فيه الحرة، وهو شهر ونصف في الطلاق بعد الدخول وشهران وخمس أيام في الوفاة، وعليه إجماع الأمة. [تبيين الحقائق: ٢٥٢/٣] لأن الشهر يتجزأ والرق يتنصف ولا فرق في ذلك في القنة وغيرها عند أبي حنيفة؛ لوجود الرق في الكل. (مستخلص، عيني)
والحامل وضعه [ أي عدة الحامل سواء كانت حرة أو أمة أو مطلقة أو متوفى عنها زوجها. (ط)] أي عدة الحامل أن تضع حملها سواء كانت في عدة الطلاق أو الفسخ أو في عدة الوفاة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُولاتُ الْأَحْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَنْ يَضَعْنَ حَمْلَهُنَّ (الطلاق:٤)، هو قول ابن مسعود وعمر ما، وقال علي الله: عدتها أبعد الأجلين؛ لأن النصوص متعارضة، بعضها يوجب تربص ثلاثة قروء كما في سورة البقرة، وبعضها أربعة أشهر وعشراً كما فيها أيضاً، وبعضها وضع الحمل كما في سورة الطلاق، فقلنا: بوجوب الأبعد احتياطاً، والجواب: أن آية الحمل متأخرة فيكون غيرها منسوخاً بها أو مخصوصاً، قال ابن مسعود الله: "من شاء باهلته أن سورة النساء القصرى نزلت بعد أربعة أشهر وعشر"، رواه أبوداود والنسائي وابن ماجه. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٩٦/١] يريد أن قوله: ﴿وَأُولاتُ الْأَحْمَالِ ) متأخر عن قوله: يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنْفُسِهِنَّ ﴾ (البقرة:۲۲۸)، فيكون ناسخاً في ذوات الأحمال. (مستخلص، فتح) وزوجة الفار أبعد الأجلين: أي عدة زوجة المريض الذي مات في مرضه وطلقها بائناً أو ثلاثاً فعدتها أبعد الأجلين من عدة الوفاة ومن عدة الطلاق، يعني إن كانت تحيض ثلاث حيض في أقل من أربعة أشهر وعشر اعتدت بأربعة أشهر وعشر؛ لأنها أطول من ثلاث حيض، وإن كانت تحيض ثلاث حيض في أكثر ذلك من اعتدت بالحيض، وهذا عند الطرفين، وعند أبي يوسف: تعتد عدة الطلاق وهو القياس؛ لأن النكاح زال بالطلاق وبقي في حق الإرث حكماً احتياطاً؛ لإجماع الصحابة، وجه الاستحسان على قولهما: أنها لما ورثت جعل قائماً حكماً إلى الوفاة؛ إذ لا إرث إلا به، فكذا في حق العدة، بل أولى، أما إذا كان الطلاق رجعياً فعليها عدة الوفاة إجماعاً [ رمز الحقائق ٢٩٦/١](مستخلص، فتح)
النكاح
انتقلت
عتقت إلخ: يعني إذا طلق امرأته الأمة طلاقاً رجعياً، ثم أعتقها المولى في العدة ومن من الطلاق الرجعي عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر؛ لأن النكاح باق من كل وجه، لا فيما إذا عتقت في عدة الطلاق البائن، ولا في عدة موت الزوج؛ لزوال النكاح ولم يتكامل الملك بعده والطلاق في الملك الناقص لا يوجب عدة الحرائر، فلا تنتقل عدتها إليها، بخلاف ما لو آلى منها ثم أبانها ثم أعتقها سيدها في العدة، حيث تصير مدة إيلائها مدة إيلاء الحرائر، ولا فرق فيه بين البائن والرجعي؛ لأن البينونة ليست من أحكام الإيلاء، فالبائن والرجعي فيه سواء بخلاف العدة، فإن سببها الطلاق وهي تعقبه فيعتبر فيها صفته، وقال مالك له: لا تزاد بالعتق، أي عدتها حيضتان كما كانت، وقال الشافعي له: عدتها عدة الحرائر في البائن والموت أيضاً. (عيني، مسكين)
And the slave woman: two menstrual cycles: meaning the iddah of a slave woman in divorce and annulment is two menstruations if she menstruates; because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two divorces and her iddah is two menstruations', and because slavery is half (of freedom) and a menstruation cannot be divided, so two complete menstruations. Umar said: If I could, I would have made it one and a half menstruations. This applies to divorce after consummation for those who menstruate, whether she is a concubine, mudabbira, maktuba, umm walad, or partially freed. (Fath, 'Ayni) 

And half of the prescribed period: meaning if she is one who does not menstruate due to youth or old age or if her husband died, then half of what is prescribed for a free woman, which is one and a half months in divorce after consummation and two months and five days in death, and upon this is the consensus of the Ummah. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 3/252] Because a month can be divided and slavery is half, and there is no difference in this between a qinn and others according to Abu Hanifa, because slavery exists in all. (Mustakhlas, 'Ayni) 

And the pregnant woman: her delivery [meaning the iddah of a pregnant woman, whether free or slave, divorced or widowed. (T)] meaning the iddah of a pregnant woman is to deliver her pregnancy whether she is in iddah of divorce, annulment, or death; because of Allah's saying: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their pregnancy' (At-Talaq:4). This is the saying of Ibn Mas'ud and Umar, and Ali said: Her iddah is the longer of the two terms; because the texts conflict, some requiring three menstrual periods as in Surat al-Baqarah, some four months and ten as in it, and some delivery as in Surat at-Talaq, so we said: the longer is required as a precaution. The answer: The verse of pregnancy is later, so others are abrogated or specified by it. Ibn Mas'ud said: 'Whoever wishes, I will debate him that the shorter Surah an-Nisa was revealed after four months and ten,' reported by Abu Dawood, an-Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/296] He meant that His saying 'And those who are pregnant' came after His saying 'They shall wait with themselves' (Al-Baqarah:228), so it is abrogating for pregnant women. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 

And the wife of the farer (the man who fled): the longer of the two terms: meaning the iddah of the wife of a sick man who died in his illness and divorced her irrevocably or three times, her iddah is the longer of the two terms from the iddah of death and the iddah of divorce. That is, if she menstruates three cycles in less than four months and ten, she observes four months and ten because it is longer than three cycles; if she menstruates three cycles in more than that, she observes iddah by menstruation. This is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf, she observes the iddah of divorce, which is the analogy, because the marriage ended by divorce and remains in effect for inheritance only as a judicial precaution due to the consensus of the Companions. The istihsan reasoning according to them is that since she inherits, the marriage is considered effective until death, because there is no inheritance without it, so likewise for iddah, even more. If the divorce was revocable, she observes the iddah of death by consensus. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/296] (Mustakhlas, Fath) 

'And whoever is freed' etc.: meaning if he divorces his slave wife with a revocable divorce, then the owner frees her during the iddah, then her iddah changes to that of free women, because the marriage remains in every respect. Not so if she is freed during the iddah of an irrevocable divorce or during the iddah of death of the husband, because the marriage has ended and complete ownership is not achieved afterwards, and divorce under incomplete ownership does not require the iddah of free women, so her iddah does not change. This is different from if he pronounced ila' on her and then divorced her irrevocably and then her master freed her during the iddah, where the period of ila' becomes the period of ila' of free women, and there is no difference in that between irrevocable and revocable, because the final separation is not part of the rulings of ila', so they are equal. Malik said: She is not increased by freedom, i.e., her iddah remains two cycles as before. Shafi'i said: Her iddah is that of free women in irrevocable divorce and death as well. ('Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings on iddah. First, for a slave woman, the iddah after divorce is two menstrual cycles based on the hadith and the principle that slavery halves the iddah. Umar's statement indicates he would have made it one and a half if possible. This applies to all types of slaves (qinn, mudabbira, etc.). Second, for a slave woman who does not menstruate or whose husband dies, the iddah is half of the prescribed period for a free woman: one and a half months for divorce, two months and five days for death. Third, for any pregnant woman (free or slave, divorced or widowed), her iddah ends with delivery, based on the Quran (65:4). The text refutes Ali's view of the longer term by stating that the pregnancy verse abrogates the earlier verses. Fourth, the case of a woman whose husband divorced her in his illness (farer) – her iddah is the longer of the death iddah and divorce iddah, because she inherits. Abu Yusuf differs, saying the iddah should be based on divorce, but the preferred view (istihsan) by the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is the longer term. Fifth, if a slave woman is freed during a revocable divorce iddah, her iddah changes to that of a free woman. If it is during an irrevocable divorce or death iddah, it does not change. This is different from the case of ila'. The passage also notes the views of Malik (no change) and Shafi'i (change in both irrevocable and death).

Key Terms
فارّ (far): A man who divorced his wife during a deadly illness to deprive her of inheritance

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the iddah of a pregnant woman according to the Hanafi school?
A) Three menstrual cycles
B) Four months and ten days
C) Until she delivers
D) The longer of delivery and three cycles

Key Takeaway
A pregnant woman's iddah ends with delivery; the wife of a farer observes the longer of death and divorce iddah to protect inheritance.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Emancipation of a Slave Woman during Iddah
Explanation of effect of emancipation on iddah of a slave woman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a slave woman's iddah changes upon emancipation, distinguishing between revocable, irrevocable, and death iddah.

Core Idea
Emancipation during a revocable divorce iddah upgrades the iddah to that of a free woman; in irrevocable divorce or death iddah, it does not change.

This lesson explains the effect of emancipation on a slave woman's iddah. The key distinction is the nature of the divorce: if the divorce was revocable, the marriage is still intact, so freedom upgrades her iddah. If the divorce was irrevocable or her husband died, the marriage is already dissolved, and emancipation does not change the iddah. This is contrasted with the case of ila'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
A slave woman can be freed during her iddah after: (a) revocable divorce, (b) irrevocable divorce, or (c) death of husband.
2
Understand the rationale for revocable divorce
In revocable divorce, the marriage is still in effect, so when she becomes free, her iddah changes to that of a free woman (three cycles or three months).
3
Understand the rationale for irrevocable and death
In irrevocable divorce or death, the marriage has ended completely. Emancipation does not revive the marriage, so her iddah remains that of a slave.
4
Contrast with the case of ila'
If a husband pronounces ila' (vow of abstinence) and then divorces irrevocably, and she is freed during iddah, her period of ila' becomes that of a free woman, because ila' rulings are not tied to the ongoing marriage in the same way.
5
Note cross-madhhab views
Malik holds that emancipation never changes the iddah. Shafi'i holds that it always changes to free iddah. The Hanafi position is intermediate.

انتقلت
عتقت إلخ: يعني إذا طلق امرأته الأمة طلاقاً رجعياً، ثم أعتقها المولى في العدة ومن من الطلاق الرجعي عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر؛ لأن النكاح باق من كل وجه، لا فيما إذا عتقت في عدة الطلاق البائن، ولا في عدة موت الزوج؛ لزوال النكاح ولم يتكامل الملك بعده والطلاق في الملك الناقص لا يوجب عدة الحرائر، فلا تنتقل عدتها إليها، بخلاف ما لو آلى منها ثم أبانها ثم أعتقها سيدها في العدة، حيث تصير مدة إيلائها مدة إيلاء الحرائر، ولا فرق فيه بين البائن والرجعي؛ لأن البينونة ليست من أحكام الإيلاء، فالبائن والرجعي فيه سواء بخلاف العدة، فإن سببها الطلاق وهي تعقبه فيعتبر فيها صفته، وقال مالك له: لا تزاد بالعتق، أي عدتها حيضتان كما كانت، وقال الشافعي له: عدتها عدة الحرائر في البائن والموت أيضاً. Commentary
This passage covers several rulings on iddah. First, for a slave woman, the iddah after divorce is two menstrual cycles based on the hadith and the principle that slavery halves the iddah. Umar's statement indicates he would have made it one and a half if possible. This applies to all types of slaves (qinn, mudabbira, etc.). Second, for a slave woman who does not menstruate or whose husband dies, the iddah is half of the prescribed period for a free woman: one and a half months for divorce, two months and five days for death. Third, for any pregnant woman (free or slave, divorced or widowed), her iddah ends with delivery, based on the Quran (65:4). The text refutes Ali's view of the longer term by stating that the pregnancy verse abrogates the earlier verses. Fourth, the case of a woman whose husband divorced her in his illness (farer) – her iddah is the longer of the death iddah and divorce iddah, because she inherits. Abu Yusuf differs, saying the iddah should be based on divorce, but the preferred view (istihsan) by the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is the longer term. Fifth, if a slave woman is freed during a revocable divorce iddah, her iddah changes to that of a free woman. If it is during an irrevocable divorce or death iddah, it does not change. This is different from the case of ila'. The passage also notes the views of Malik (no change) and Shafi'i (change in both irrevocable and death).

Key Terms
أمة (amah): A female slave
إيلاء (ila'): A vow by the husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife, leading to a judicial process.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case does a slave woman's iddah upgrade to that of a free woman upon emancipation?
A) During death iddah
B) During irrevocable divorce iddah
C) During revocable divorce iddah
D) In all cases

Key Takeaway
Emancipation changes a slave woman's iddah only if she was in revocable divorce iddah, because the marriage continues.

Pregnant Woman's Iddah Ends with Delivery
Ruling and proof that pregnant woman's iddah ends at delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a pregnant woman's iddah, cite the Quranic evidence, and explain the Hanafi response to the opposing view of Ali ibn Abi Talib.

Core Idea
The iddah of a pregnant woman ends with delivery, as the later Quranic verse abrogates earlier iddah rules.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on a pregnant woman's iddah. While some early scholars like Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA) held that she must observe the longer of two periods, the Hanafi school, based on a clear Quranic verse and its chronological priority, rules that delivery alone ends the iddah. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly advising women in such situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The author states that the iddah of a pregnant woman ends with delivery, regardless of whether she is free or slave, divorced or widowed.
This is the core Hanafi position.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is Quran 65:4, which explicitly says 'their term is that they deliver their pregnancy'.
The verse is clear (qati' al-dalala).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse uses the word 'ajal' (term) and 'yada'na' (deliver), indicating that the iddah is completed upon delivery, not after a fixed period.
No additional waiting is required.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA) held that the pregnant woman must wait the longer of two periods (delivery or four months and ten days) due to conflicting texts.
His view is based on precaution (ihtiyat).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school responds that the pregnancy verse is later (nasikh) and thus abrogates the earlier iddah verses. Ibn Mas'ud confirms this chronological order.
Therefore the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text

And the pregnant woman, her delivery (i.e., the iddah of a pregnant woman is that she gives birth to her pregnancy, whether free or slave, divorced or widowed; because of His saying: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their pregnancy' (Surah Talaq:4). This is the saying of Ibn Mas'ud and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them). And Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: her iddah is the longer of the two periods because the texts conflict; some require waiting three periods as in Surah Al-Baqarah, and some require four months and ten as also in it, and some require delivery as in Surah Al-Talaq. So we said: the longer is obligatory as a precaution. The answer is that the verse of pregnancy is later, so other verses are abrogated by it or specified. Ibn Mas'ud said: 'Whoever wishes, I will imprecate that the short Surah of Women was revealed after four months and ten days'—reported by Abu Dawood, Al-Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah. He meant that His saying 'And those who are pregnant' is later than His saying 'They shall wait with themselves for three periods' (Surah Al-Baqarah:228), so it abrogates for pregnant women.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the iddah of a pregnant woman. The author states that the iddah of a pregnant woman, whether free or slave, divorced or widowed, ends with the delivery of her child. The primary proof is the Quranic verse in Surah Al-Talaq (65:4). The author then presents a dispute: Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA) held that the iddah should be the longer of the two periods (three menstrual cycles or four months and ten days or delivery) as a precaution because of conflicting texts. The Hanafi position, following Ibn Mas'ud and Umar, is that the verse on pregnancy abrogates all other iddah rules for pregnant women. The author provides a response to Ali's view: the pregnancy verse is later in revelation, thus it overrides earlier verses. The hadith from Ibn Mas'ud confirms this chronological order.

Key Terms
عدة الحامل (iddat al-hamil): The waiting period for a pregnant woman after divorce or death; it ends upon delivery.

Iddah of the Wife of a Terminally Ill Divorcer (Farr)
Ruling on iddah of woman divorced by terminally ill husband who dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of a woman divorced by a terminally ill husband (al-farr) and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, including the role of qiyas and istihsan.

Core Idea
When a terminally ill husband divorces and dies, the wife's iddah is the longer of death's iddah (4 months 10 days) or divorce's iddah (3 cycles).

This lesson examines a nuanced case where a terminally ill man divorces his wife irrevocably and then dies from that illness. The Hanafi school has two views: the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) require the longer of two iddahs as a precaution, while Abu Yusuf applies strict analogy (qiyas) to the regular divorce iddah. We will walk through their arguments and determine the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two Hanafi positions
Identify the scenario: wife divorced ba'in or thrice by terminally ill husband who then dies. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rule the longer of the two iddahs; Abu Yusuf rules only the divorce iddah.
2
Analyze Abu Yusuf's qiyas argument
Abu Yusuf analogizes to a healthy husband's divorce: marriage ends, so iddah of divorce applies. He sees inheritance as a separate legal fiction (hukman) for precaution.
His asl: woman divorced by healthy husband → iddah of divorce. Far: same but husband dies. Illa: marriage ended (zawal al-nikah).
3
Analyze the majority's istihsan argument
The majority argue that since she inherits (indicating marriage is considered ongoing until death for financial purposes), the same logic applies to iddah. They use istihsan (preferring a stronger effect) over qiyas.
They say: if marriage exists for inheritance, it should also exist for iddah, perhaps more so (bal awla).
4
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical effect
The relied-upon position is the majority view: longer of the two periods. This means the actual iddah depends on which period ends later: if 3 cycles take more than 4m10d, she observes 3 cycles; otherwise 4m10d.
5
Note the consensus on revocable divorce
If the divorce was revocable (raj'i), there is consensus that she observes the iddah of death (4m10d).
Because the marriage is still ongoing during the revocable period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزوجة الفار أبعد الأجلين: أي عدة زوجة المريض الذي مات في مرضه وطلقها بائناً أو ثلاثاً فعدتها أبعد الأجلين من عدة الوفاة ومن عدة الطلاق، يعني إن كانت تحيض ثلاث حيض في أقل من أربعة أشهر وعشر اعتدت بأربعة أشهر وعشر؛ لأنها أطول من ثلاث حيض، وإن كانت تحيض ثلاث حيض في أكثر ذلك من اعتدت بالحيض، وهذا عند الطرفين، وعند أبي يوسف: تعتد عدة الطلاق وهو القياس؛ لأن النكاح زال بالطلاق وبقي في حق الإرث حكماً احتياطاً؛ لإجماع الصحابة، وجه الاستحسان على قولهما: أنها لما ورثت جعل قائماً حكماً إلى الوفاة؛ إذ لا إرث إلا به، فكذا في حق العدة، بل أولى، أما إذا كان الطلاق رجعياً فعليها عدة الوفاة إجماعاً
And the wife of the farr (terminally ill divorcer) [has] the longer of the two periods: i.e., the iddah of the wife of the sick man who dies in his illness and divorces her with a ba'in or triple divorce; her iddah is the longer of the two periods: the iddah of death and the iddah of divorce. That is, if she menstruates three times in less than four months and ten days, she observes four months and ten days because it is longer than three menstruations; and if she menstruates three times in more than that, she observes by menstruation. This is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf: she observes the iddah of divorce, and this is analogy because the marriage ended by divorce and only remains for inheritance as a precaution due to the consensus of the Companions. The basis of istihsan according to them is that since she inherits, the marriage is considered existing until death, for inheritance only occurs with it, so likewise regarding iddah, rather more so. As for a revocable divorce, she observes the iddah of death by consensus.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the iddah of a woman whose husband divorces her with a irrevocable (ba'in) or triple divorce while terminally ill, and then dies. The ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the 'two') is that she must observe the longer of the two possible iddahs: four months and ten days (iddah of death) or three menstrual cycles (iddah of divorce), whichever ends later. Abu Yusuf holds she only observes the iddah of divorce (three cycles), which is the qiyas (analogy) because the marriage has ended. However, the majority use istihsan (juristic preference) based on the fact that she inherits, indicating the marriage is considered ongoing legally until death, so the iddah should reflect that. If the divorce was revocable (raj'i), there is consensus she observes the iddah of death (four months ten days).

Key Terms
الفار (al-farr): A man who divorces his wife while terminally ill and then dies from that illness.
عدة الوفاة (iddat al-wafat): The waiting period observed by a widow: four months and ten days.
عدة الطلاق (iddat al-talaq): The waiting period for a divorced woman: three menstrual cycles (or three months for menopausal women).
أبعد الأجلين (ab'ad al-ajalayn): The longer of the two periods (iddah of death vs. iddah of divorce).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Three menstrual cycles
B) Four months and ten days
C) The longer of A and B
D) No iddah because the divorce ended the marriage

Key Takeaway
The iddah of a woman divorced by a terminally ill husband who dies is the longer of the two periods, a precautionary measure that protects her rights and ensures clarity of paternity.

Iddah of a Slave Woman Freed During Iddah: Hanafi Distinction by Divorce Type
Ruling on change of iddah for slave woman freed during waiting period, with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of a slave woman freed during iddah, distinguish between raj'i and ba'in divorce, and evaluate the Shafi'i and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
Freedom upgrades iddah only when the marriage remains intact — in raj'i divorce, not in ba'in or death.

This lesson examines a nuanced fiqh issue: when a slave woman is freed during her waiting period, does her iddah become that of a free woman? The Hanafi school provides a conditional answer based on the type of divorce, contrasting with the Maliki and Shafi'i positions. Understanding this distinction reveals how the school links the iddah ruling to the legal continuity of marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Recognize the scenario: a slave woman is divorced and during her iddah she is freed by her master. The question is whether her iddah changes from that of a slave (two cycles) to that of a free woman (three cycles).
2
State the Hanafi position and the condition
The Hanafi school holds that her iddah becomes that of a free woman only if the divorce was revocable (raj'i). If the divorce was irrevocable (ba'in) or due to the husband's death, her iddah remains that of a slave.
The key condition is the continuity of marriage (baqa' al-nikah).
3
Explain the reasoning (ta'lil)
In raj'i divorce, the marriage is still fully intact, so freedom enhances her status and her iddah reflects that. In ba'in or death, the marriage is severed, and since she did not possess full property (milk) after freedom, the original slave iddah remains. The cause (sabab) of iddah is divorce, and its nature determines the iddah.
Contrast with ila': in ila' the separation is not a ruling of ila', so freedom affects it regardless of divorce type.
4
Contrast with opposing schools
Imam Malik holds that freedom never increases the iddah; it remains two cycles. Imam Shafi'i holds that freedom always increases the iddah to that of a free woman, even in ba'in and death.
Hanafi position is intermediate: only when marriage is intact.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a slave woman whose husband makes ila' (vow of abstinence), then divorces her irrevocably, then she is freed during iddah. Hanafi: her ila' period becomes that of a free woman (four months) because ila' rulings are different from iddah rulings.
This illustrates the consistency of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النكاح انتقلت عتقت إلخ: يعني إذا طلق امرأته الأمة طلاقاً رجعياً، ثم أعتقها المولى في العدة ومن من الطلاق الرجعي عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر؛ لأن النكاح باق من كل وجه، لا فيما إذا عتقت في عدة الطلاق البائن، ولا في عدة موت الزوج؛ لزوال النكاح ولم يتكامل الملك بعده والطلاق في الملك الناقص لا يوجب عدة الحرائر، فلا تنتقل عدتها إليها، بخلاف ما لو آلى منها ثم أبانها ثم أعتقها سيدها في العدة، حيث تصير مدة إيلائها مدة إيلاء الحرائر، ولا فرق فيه بين البائن والرجعي؛ لأن البينونة ليست من أحكام الإيلاء، فالبائن والرجعي فيه سواء بخلاف العدة، فإن سببها الطلاق وهي تعقبه فيعتبر فيها صفته، وقال مالك له: لا تزاد بالعتق، أي عدتها حيضتان كما كانت، وقال الشافعي له: عدتها عدة الحرائر في البائن والموت أيضاً.
في عدّة الرجعي لا الباثن والموت كالحرّة: أي الطلاق الرجعي فعدتها كعدة الحرة؛ لأن النكاح باق عن كل وجه فوجب انتقال عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر. (ع) لا البائن أي لا إذا أعتقت في عدة البائن. (ع) والموت أي ولا في عدة موت الزوج لزوال النكاح. (ع) كالحرة في محل الرفع على أنه خبر عن قوله : ومن عتقت أي عدتها كعدة الحرة. (ع)
The marriage, transition, freed, etc.: meaning if a man divorces his slave woman with a revocable divorce, then her master frees her during the iddah, her iddah transfers to that of a free woman because the marriage is intact from every aspect. Not so if she is freed during the iddah of an irrevocable divorce, nor during the iddah of the husband's death, because the marriage is gone and full ownership did not occur thereafter, and divorce under incomplete ownership does not require the iddah of a free woman. Therefore her iddah does not transfer to it. Unlike if he made ila' then divorced her irrevocably then her master freed her during iddah, where the period of ila' becomes that of a free woman, and there is no difference between ba'in and raj'i in that because separation is not a ruling of ila', so ba'in and raj'i are the same in it, unlike iddah, because its cause is divorce and it follows it, so its nature is considered. Malik said: she does not increase by freedom; her iddah remains two menstrual cycles as before. Shafi'i said: her iddah becomes that of a free woman in ba'in and death as well.
In the iddah of raj'i, not ba'in or death, she is like a free woman: i.e., revocable divorce, so her iddah is like that of a free woman because the marriage is intact from every aspect, thus her iddah must transfer to that of a free woman. Not ba'in, i.e., not if she is freed during the iddah of an irrevocable divorce. And death, i.e., not during the iddah of the husband's death due to the end of marriage. This is the ruling from the one who says: her iddah is like that of a free woman.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the effect of freeing a slave woman during her iddah, with inter-madhhab comparisons. The Hanafi position distinguishes based on the type of divorce. If the divorce was revocable (raj'i), the marriage is considered fully intact, so upon freedom her iddah becomes that of a free woman (three menstrual cycles). If the divorce was irrevocable (ba'in) or her husband died, the marriage is ended, and since she did not own full property after freedom, her iddah remains that of a slave (two cycles). The author contrasts this with the case of ila' (vow of abstinence), where freedom affects the period regardless of divorce type. Imam Malik holds freedom never increases iddah; Imam Shafi'i holds it increases to free woman's iddah in all cases (ba'in and death). This shows the Hanafi position is intermediate.

Key Terms
عدة الأمة (iddat al-amah): The waiting period of a female slave; normally half that of a free woman (two cycles).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Irrevocable (ba'in) divorce

D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the iddah of a freed slave woman increases to that of a free woman only in revocable divorce because the marriage is fully intact.

Months-Based Iddah Invalidated by Return of Menstruation
Ruling on return of menstruation during months-based iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a menopausal woman who menstruates during her iddah by months and explain the rationale using the concept of substitute (khalaf).

Core Idea
When menstruation returns, the months-based iddah is invalidated and she restarts with cycles — the substitute falls when the original is possible.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in iddah: a woman thought to be menopausal begins counting iddah by months, but then menstruates. The Hanafi school rules that her months-based iddah becomes void, and she must restart with menstrual cycles. The reasoning hinges on the principle that months are a substitute for cycles, and a substitute is only valid when the original is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial assumption
The woman is assumed menopausal (ayisah) and thus starts iddah by months (the substitute). Note that months are only valid when she cannot menstruate.
Menopause (iyas) is defined as the cessation of menstruation; the majority Hanafi view sets the age at 55.
2
Recognize the critical event: return of menstruation
During her months-based iddah, she sees blood consistent with her habitual cycle. This blood indicates that she is not truly menopausal.
The text specifies 'if she sees blood according to her habit' – meaning it is genuine menstruation, not irregular spotting.
3
Apply the principle: substitute (khalaf) falls when original is possible
Months are a substitute for menstrual cycles. The substitute is valid only as long as the original is impossible. If the original becomes possible again, the substitute is void.
Analogous to an elderly person who cannot fast and pays ransom, but if he regains ability, the ransom no longer suffices.
4
Determine the legal consequence
Her months-based iddah is invalidated entirely. She must restart iddah by observing three menstrual cycles (quru').
The previous months counted do not count at all; she starts afresh.
5
Consider related cases for contrast
If she becomes pregnant during the months-based iddah, the same invalidation occurs. However, if a young girl (not yet menopausal) menstruates after completing months-based iddah, she does not restart – because it does not prove she was capable of cycles during the iddah period.
The key is timing: if menstruation occurs during the iddah period, it shows the substitute was invalid from the start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عاد دَمُها : [ أي من أيست فعدت بالأشهر ثم عاد. (و)] أي إذا كانت المرأة آيسة فاعتدت بالشهور، ثم رأت الدم انتقض ما مضى من عدتها وعليها أن تستأنفها بالحيض، معناه إذا رأت الدم على عادتها؛ لأن الدم يبطل الإياس، هو الصحيح، فظهر أنه لم يكن خلفاً؛ لأن شرط الخلفية تحقق الإياس عن الأصل، وذلك باستدامة العجز إلى الموت كالفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني، وكذلك إذا حبلت من زوج آخر انتقضت عدتها وفسد نكاحها؛ لأنه تبين أنها من ذوات الأقراء؛ إذ الآيسة لا تحبل والصغيرة إذا حاضت بعد انقضاء عدتها بالأشهر لا تستأنف؛ لأنه لم يتبين أنها كانت من ذوات الأقراء، بخلاف ما إذا حاضت في أثناء العدة حيث تستأنف تحرزاً عن الجمع بين الأصل والبدل. رمز الحقائق: ۲۹۷/۱] ، لأن الاعتداد بالأشهر خلف عن بالحيض فلا يجوز تكميل أحدهما بالآخر. واختلف في سن الإياس، وحدّها أن تبلغ حداً لا تحيض فيه مثلها، وقال بعضهم: يعتبر بقرابتها، وقيل: بتركيب بدنها من السمن والهزال، وعند الأكثرين : خمس وخمسون سنة وعليه الفتوى وهو قول عائشة لها وسفيان الثوري وابن المبارك وابن مقاتل الرازي (فتح) الحيض: أي فعدتها الحيض، وانتقض ما مضى من عدتها.
And from it: one whose blood returns: (i.e., a woman who is menopausal and observed iddah by months, then blood returned). If a woman is menopausal and observed iddah by months, then sees blood, whatever has passed of her iddah is invalidated and she must restart it with menstruation. This means if she sees blood according to her habit, because blood nullifies menopause. This is the correct (opinion), so it becomes clear that it was not a substitute (khalaf), because the condition of substitution is the establishment of menopause from the original, and that is by persistence of inability until death, like the ransom for the elderly (al-fani). Similarly, if she becomes pregnant from another husband, her iddah is invalidated and her marriage becomes corrupt, because it becomes clear that she is of those with menstrual cycles, since a menopausal woman does not become pregnant. And a young girl who menstruates after completing her iddah by months does not restart, because it is not apparent that she was of those with cycles, unlike if she menstruated during the iddah where she restarts to avoid combining original and substitute. Because observing iddah by months is a substitute for by menstruation, so it is not permissible to complete one with the other. And they differed on the age of menopause; its definition is that she reaches an age where someone like her does not menstruate. Some said it depends on relatives; it was said based on body composition (fatness or thinness); according to the majority: fifty-five years, and upon it is the fatwa, and it is the saying of Aisha (RA), Sufyan al-Thawri, Ibn al-Mubarak, and Ibn Muqatil al-Razi. (Fath) Menstruation: i.e., her iddah is by menstruation, and what passed of her iddah is invalidated.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the case of a woman who was thought to be menopausal (ayisah) and began her iddah by counting months, but then menstruation returns during that period. The Hanafi ruling: she must abandon the months and restart her iddah with menstrual cycles, because blood indicates she was not truly menopausal. The author justifies this by stating that the months-based iddah is a substitute (khalaf) for the original (menstrual cycle), and a substitute is only valid when the original is impossible; if the original becomes possible again, the substitute is void. Similar logic applies if she becomes pregnant from another man during iddah: it shows she is capable of pregnancy, so months-based iddah is invalid. The passage also discusses the definition of menopause: the majority holds it is 55 years (the age after which women typically stop menstruating), and this is the fatwa position.

Key Terms
الإياس (al-iyas): The condition of a woman who has permanently ceased menstruation due to age.
الخلف (al-khalaf): Substitute – a legal alternative used when the original obligation is permanently impossible.
الأقراء (al-aqra'): Menstrual periods (or the intervals between them); used to measure iddah for non-pregnant women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She continues with months but adds one cycle.
B) She restarts her iddah with menstrual cycles.
C) She must wait until menopause is confirmed again.
D) She can choose between months and cycles.

Key Takeaway
When a woman in months-based iddah menstruates, her iddah is reset and she must observe three menstrual cycles from the beginning.

Slave Woman's 'Iddah: Raj'i Exception and Exceptions
Stating the ruling for slave woman's iddah and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a slave woman freed during 'iddah and identify the two exceptions (ba'in and death) with the reason based on the marriage's status.

Core Idea
A slave woman freed during a revocable divorce adopts the 'iddah of a free woman; exceptions: irrevocable divorce and death.

This lesson clarifies a specific rule in the Hanafi law of 'iddah: when a slave woman is freed during her waiting period, her 'iddah changes to that of a free woman—but not always. The key is the nature of the divorce. Understanding this distinction helps you apply the correct period in real cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Locate the core statement: a slave woman freed during 'iddah has the same 'iddah as a free woman. This is the default rule.
The text states: 'عدة المملوكة كالحرة' — her 'iddah is like that of a free woman.
2
Identify the exceptions
Find the two cases where the default does NOT apply: ba'in divorce and death of husband. The text says 'إلا في البائن والموت'.
These are the only exceptions mentioned.
3
Understand the rationale for the exception
For raj'i divorce, the marriage bond persists, so manumission during 'iddah elevates her to free status. For ba'in and death, the marriage is severed, so no transfer occurs.
The commentator explains: in raj'i 'النكاح باق عن كل وجه' — the marriage remains in all respects.
4
Parse the grammatical structure
The phrase 'كالحرة' is a predicate (khabar) of the implied subject 'من أعتقت' (whoever is freed). This is a parsing exercise to ensure correct reading.
The abbreviation (ع) likely refers to a commentator like al-'Ayni.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العدة في عدّة الرجعي لا البائن والموت كالحرة الرجعي: أي الطلاق الرجعي فعدتها كعدة الحرة؛ لأن النكاح باق عن كل وجه فوجب انتقال عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر. (ع) لا البائن أي لا إذا أعتقت في عدة البائن. (ع) والموت أي ولا في عدة موت الزوج لزوال النكاح. (ع) كالحرة في محل الرفع على أنه خبر عن قوله : ومن عتقت أي عدتها كعدة الحرة. (ع)
Chapter of Iddah: Regarding the iddah of the raj'i (divorce) not the ba'in, and death, it is like that of a free woman. The raj'i: meaning the raj'i divorce, so her iddah is like the iddah of a free woman because the marriage remains in every aspect, so her iddah must transfer to the iddah of free women. (A) Not the ba'in: meaning not if she is freed during the iddah of ba'in. (A) And death: meaning not in the iddah of the husband's death because the marriage is removed. (A) 'Like a free woman' is in the nominative case as a predicate of His statement: 'And whoever is freed' i.e., her iddah is like the iddah of a free woman. (A)
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The chapter on 'iddah begins here with the case of a slave woman who is freed during her 'iddah. The matn states that her 'iddah becomes like that of a free woman, except in two cases: (1) if the divorce was ba'in (irrevocable), and (2) if the husband has died. The commentary explains that in raj'i (revocable) divorce, the marriage bond remains intact in all respects, so the 'iddah of a free woman applies after manumission. In ba'in divorce, the marriage has already been severed, so manumission during that 'iddah does not change the 'iddah. Similarly, upon the death of the husband, the marriage is completely terminated, so no transfer occurs. The phrase 'like a free woman' is the predicate of the implied subject 'whoever is freed' (man uttiqat). The abbreviation (ع) likely refers to a commentator, possibly al-'Ayni. This ruling is part of the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position. There is no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf mentioned here.

Key Terms
الرجعي (al-raj'ī): A revocable divorce where the husband may take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new contract.
البائن (al-bā'in): An irrevocable divorce after which the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.
العدة (al-'iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which type of divorce does a slave woman freed during 'iddah NOT change to a free woman's 'iddah?
A) Raj'i only
B) Ba'in only
C) Both ba'in and death
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
A slave woman freed during 'iddah assumes the 'iddah of a free woman only if the divorce was raj'i; the exceptions are ba'in and death due to the termination of marriage.

Return of Menses After Menopausal Iddah by Months
Ruling with proof on nullification of monthly iddah when menses return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a menopausal woman's menses return after completing iddah by months, and explain the rationale of substitution (khalaf).

Core Idea
If menses return after a menopausal iddah by months, the months are void: the original cancels the substitute.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary expands on a crucial principle: when a woman relies on a substitute (counting months) because of menopause, but her menses return, the substitute collapses. The key is that a substitute is only valid as long as the original is impossible. Once the original becomes possible, the substitute loses its basis. Let's explore the ruling, its rationale, and the conditions under which the iddah must be restarted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The woman is menopausal (al-iyas) and has counted three months as her iddah after a revocable divorce.
Menopause in Hanafi fiqh is typically defined as cessation of menses at age 55.
2
Apply the trigger
She then sees blood according to her usual menstrual habit. This blood negates the state of menopause.
Irregular spotting does not trigger this ruling; it must be genuine menstrual blood.
3
Understand the consequence
The months already counted are nullified. She must start her iddah anew with three menstrual cycles.
She cannot combine the months already counted with future menses; the substitute is void ab initio.
4
Grasp the legal principle
Counting months is a substitute (khalaf) for menses. A substitute is only valid when the original is impossible. Returning menses proves the original was possible all along.
The text compares this to fidyah for an elderly person who cannot fast: if they later become able, the fidyah is invalid.
5
Note the distinctions
A minor who menstruates after completing iddah by months does NOT restart, because she was never truly menopausal. However, if she menstruates during the iddah, she must restart to avoid combining original and substitute.
The age of menopause is debated; the relied-upon view is 55 years.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عاد دمها: [ أي من أيست فعدت بالأشهر ثم عاد. (و)] أي إذا كانت المرأة آيسة فاعتدت بالشهور، ثم رأت الدم انتقض ما مضى من عدتها وعليها أن تستأنفها بالحيض، معناه إذا رأت الدم على عادتها؛ لأن الدم يبطل الإياس، هو الصحيح، فظهر أنه لم يكن خلفاً؛ لأن شرط الخلفية تحقق الإياس عن الأصل، وذلك باستدامة العجز إلى الموت كالفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني، وكذلك إذا حبلت من زوج آخر انتقضت عدتها وفسد نكاحها؛ لأنه تبين أنها كانت من ذوات الأقراء؛ إذ الآيسة لا تحبل والصغيرة إذا حاضت بعد انقضاء عدتها بالأشهر لا تستأنف؛ لأنه لم يتبين أنها كانت من ذوات الأقراء، بخلاف ما إذا حاضت في أثناء العدة حيث تستأنف تحرزاً عن الجمع بين الأصل والبدل. رمز الحقائق: ۲۹۷/۱]، لأن الاعتداد بالأشهر خلف عن بالحيض فلا يجوز تكميل أحدهما بالآخر. واختلف في سن الإياس، وحدّها أن تبلغ حداً لا تحيض فيه مثلها، وقال بعضهم: يعتبر بقرابتها، وقيل: بتركيب بدنها من السمن والهزال، وعند الأكثرين : خمس وخمسون سنة وعليه الفتوى وهو قول عائشة لها وسفيان الثوري وابن المبارك وابن مقاتل الرازي.
And she whose blood returns: [i.e., she who became menopausal and counted [her iddah] by months, then it returned. (W)] That is, if a woman is menopausal and she counted [the iddah] by months, then she sees blood, what has passed of her iddah is nullified and she must start it anew with menses. The meaning is if she sees blood according to her usual habit, because blood negates menopause. This is the correct [view]. So it appears that it was not a substitute, because the condition for substitution is the realization of incapacity from the original, and that is by the continuation of incapacity until death, like the ransom (fidyah) in the case of the extremely old man. Similarly, if she becomes pregnant from another husband, her iddah is nullified and her marriage is corrupted, because it becomes clear that she was among those who have menses, since a menopausal woman does not become pregnant. As for a minor, if she menstruates after completing her iddah by months, she does not restart, because it is not clear that she was among those who have menses. This differs from the case if she menstruates during the iddah, where she restarts to avoid combining the original and the substitute. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 297/1] Because counting by months is a substitute for counting by menses, so it is not permissible to complete one with the other. There is disagreement about the age of menopause. Its limit is that she reaches an age where her peers do not menstruate. Some said it is considered by her relatives; some said by the composition of her body in terms of fatness and thinness. According to the majority, it is fifty-five years, and upon it is the fatwa. It is the saying of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), Sufyan al-Thawri, Ibn al-Mubarak, and Ibn Muqatil al-Razi.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage expands on a ruling from the matn regarding a woman who was menopausal and completed her 'iddah by months, but then her menses return. The correct view is that the 'iddah by months is nullified and she must start again with menses, because the return of blood proves she was never truly menopausal. The analogy is made to the fidyah for an elderly person who cannot fast: if he later becomes able, the fidyah is invalid. The same applies if she becomes pregnant from another man during the 'iddah by months—it shows she is of menstruating age, so her 'iddah is void. A minor girl, however, if she menstruates after completing her 'iddah by months, does not restart, because her earlier capability was not established. The passage also discusses the age of menopause: the majority (and relied-upon position) is 55 years, citing Aisha, Sufyan al-Thawri, etc. This ruling is based on the principle that a substitute cannot be combined with the original; once the original (menses) is possible, the substitute (months) is dropped.

Key Terms
الإياس (al-iyās): Menopause: the state where a woman no longer menstruates due to age, typically 55 years according to the relied-upon view.
الاستئناف (al-isti'nāf): Restarting the 'iddah from the beginning when the previous months are invalidated.

Key Takeaway
If a menopausal woman's menses return after completing iddah by months, her iddah is void and she must restart with menses, because the substitute (months) cannot stand when the original (menses) is possible.

Pregnant Wife of a Minor Husband and Her Iddah
Ruling on 'iddah of a minor's pregnant wife and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the 'iddah of a minor's pregnant wife and distinguish Abu Yusuf's view, including the practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
A minor's pregnant wife ends 'iddah at birth if delivery is within six months; otherwise, four months ten days.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the 'iddah of a minor's wife who is pregnant at the time of his death. The key question is whether her 'iddah is determined by delivery (as per the general Qur'anic verse) or by the fixed period for a non-pregnant widow, given that the minor cannot father a child. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad give effect to the verse when birth occurs within a plausible timeframe, while Abu Yusuf insists on the fixed period in all cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Identify the two scenarios: if she gives birth within less than six months of his death, her 'iddah ends with delivery; if after six months or more, it is four months and ten days.
The six-month threshold is based on the minimum possible pregnancy duration.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf holds that her 'iddah is always four months and ten days, regardless of when she gives birth, because a minor cannot father a child.
He treats the pregnancy as occurring after death, thus the death-iddah applies.
3
Analyze the proofs for each position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rely on the general verse (65:4) which applies to any pregnant woman. Abu Yusuf argues that since the child is not from the minor, the pregnancy is irrelevant for 'iddah.
The commentary explains that for an adult, a later pregnancy is attributed to him up to two years, implying existence at death – not so for a minor.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under the majority, a wife giving birth 5 months after death ends 'iddah at birth; under Abu Yusuf, she must wait 4 months 10 days even if she already gave birth.
This affects when she can remarry.
5
Note the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it gives effect to the clear Qur'anic text.
This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزوجة الصغير الحامل أي عدة زوجة الصغير الذي مات عن امرأته وهي حامل وقت الموت وضع حملها لعموم الآية: ﴿وَأُولاتُ الْأَحْمَالِ (الطلاق:٤)، وصورة قيام الحمل وقت الموت أن تلد بعد موته لأقل أشهر من وقت موته، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف لله والثلاثة: عدتها أربعة أشهر وعشراً؛ لأن الحمل ليس بثابت النسب منه، فصار كالحادث بعد الموت، ولهما إطلاق قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُولاتُ الْأَحْمَالِ ؛ ولأنها مقدرة بمدة وضع الحمل في ذوات الأحمال، طالت المدة أو قصرت لا للتعارف عن فراغ الرحم؛ لشرعها بالأشهر مع وجود الأقراء لقضاء حق النكاح، وهذا المعنى يتحقق في الصبي؛ لأنها حامل متوفى عنها زوجها، فعليها العدة بوضع الحمل وإن لم يكن الحمل منه. (مستخلص) موته: أي عند موت الزوج بأن تلد لأقل من ستة أشهر بعد موت الصغير. (ط) و الحامل بعده [ أي بعد الموت بأن ولدته نصف حول فأكثر . (ط)] أي عدة زوجة الصغير الحامل بعد موته أربعة أشهر وعشر؛ لأنه لم يثبت وجود الحمل وقت الموت لا حقيقة ولا حكماً، فتعينت الأشهر عند الموت، فلا تعتبر بحدوث الحمل بعد ذلك، بخلاف امرأة الكبير إذا حدث بها الحبل بعد الموت؛ لأن نسبه ثابت إلى الحولين، ومن ضرورته وجود الحمل عند الموت، فتبين أنه ليس بحادث حتى لو تيقن بحدوثه بأن ولدته بعد الحولين كان الحكم كذلك. رمز الحقائق: ۲۹۸/۱۸] (مستخلص، فتح) والنسب منتف فيهما : [ أي في الوجهين جميعا وينبغي ثبوته من المراهق احتياطاً. (ط)] أي لا يثبت نسب هذا الحمل من الصغير في كلتا الصورتين وهما الحمل الحادث عند الموت والحادث بعد الموت؛ لاستحالته الصغير؛ لأن النسب يعتمد الماء، ولا ماء له، فلا يتصور منه العلوق رمز الحقائق: ۲۹۸/۱] (مستخلص)
And the pregnant wife of the minor: i.e., the 'iddah of the wife of a minor who died leaving his wife pregnant at the time of death is her delivery of the pregnancy, due to the generality of the verse: 'And those who are pregnant' (At-Talaq:4). The scenario of the existence of pregnancy at the time of death is that she gives birth after his death in less than months from the time of his death. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: Her 'iddah is four months and ten days because the pregnancy is not of established lineage from him, so it becomes like something occurring after death. Their proof is the absolute wording of the verse: 'And those who are pregnant', and because she is determined by the period of delivery in pregnant women, whether the period is long or short, not for knowing the emptiness of the womb because the 'iddah is prescribed by months even when menses exist to fulfill the right of marriage, and this meaning applies to the minor boy because she is a pregnant woman whose husband has died, so her 'iddah is by delivery even if the pregnancy is not from him. (Mukhtasar) His death: i.e., at the death of the husband, that she gives birth less than six months after the death of the minor. (T) And the pregnant after it: [i.e., after death, if she gives birth half a year or more. (T)] i.e., the 'iddah of the pregnant wife of the minor after his death is four months and ten days, because the existence of pregnancy at the time of death is not established, neither in reality nor legally, so the months are determined at the time of death, and what occurs after is not considered. This is unlike the wife of an adult man if she becomes pregnant after death, because his lineage is established up to two years, and by necessity the pregnancy existed at death, so it is proven that it is not new. Even if it is certain that it occurred, if she gives birth after two years, the ruling is the same. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 298/18] (Mukhtasar, Fath) And the lineage is negated in both cases: [i.e., in both aspects, and it should be established from a teenager (murahiq) as a precaution. (T)] i.e., the lineage of this pregnancy is not established from the minor in both images: the pregnancy occurring at death and occurring after death, because it is impossible from a minor, because lineage depends on fluid (water), and he has no fluid, so impregnation from him is inconceivable. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 298/1] (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the 'iddah of a minor's pregnant wife. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that if she gives birth within less than six months (the minimum pregnancy duration for possibility) of the minor's death, then the 'iddah ends with delivery, applying the verse 'Ulatu al-ahmal'. If she gives birth after six months or more, then the 'iddah is four months and ten days (the 'iddah for death without pregnancy). Abu Yusuf differs, saying her 'iddah is always four months and ten days because the child is not from the minor (since a minor cannot father a child), so the pregnancy is considered to have occurred after death, and thus she is not truly pregnant at the time of death. The commentary explains that for an adult husband, if pregnancy appears after death, it is attributed to him up to two years, implying it existed at death. But for a minor, lineage is impossible, so the pregnancy is seen as new. The text also notes that the lineage of the child is not established from the minor in either case (whether born within 6 months or after), because a minor cannot produce sperm. This is a precise legal point based on biological impossibility. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it follows the general verse.

Key Terms
الصغير (al-ṣaghīr): A minor male who has not reached puberty, considered unable to father a child in Hanafi law.
وضع الحمل (waḍ' al-ḥaml): Delivery of the pregnancy; it marks the end of 'iddah for a pregnant woman according to the Qur'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Four months and ten days
B) Ends with delivery
C) Three months
D) One year

Key Takeaway
A minor's pregnant wife's 'iddah depends on the timing of birth relative to death, with the majority view distinguishing based on the six-month threshold.

Menstruation at Divorce Does Not Count
Ruling that divorce during menses does not count

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the menstruation during which divorce occurs is not counted in the 'iddah, and determine the number of complete menses required for a free woman and a slave woman.

Core Idea
The menses at divorce doesn't count – only complete cycles after count.

A common question in family law is how a woman counts her 'iddah after divorce, especially if she was menstruating at the time. This ruling clarifies that the current menstrual cycle is not included. You'll learn the basic rule and its application to free and slave women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the counting of menses in 'iddah after divorce. The ruling: the menstruation that was ongoing at the moment of divorce is not counted.
2
Recall the legal basis
The Qur'an requires three quru' (menstrual cycles) for free women and two for slave women. A cycle that already started before divorce is not a complete post-divorce cycle.
This is based on the apparent meaning of the text (nass).
3
Distinguish between free and slave women
A free woman must observe three complete menses after divorce; a slave woman observes two. The excluded menses does not count toward either number.
This distinction is explicit in the source.
4
Apply to a scenario
If a free woman is divorced on day two of her menses, she must wait for three complete menstrual cycles after that menses ends.
The current menses is disregarded entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم تعتد بحيض: أي إذا طلقت المرأة وهي حائض لا تحتسب بتلك الحيضة التي وقع فيها الطلاق؛ لأن الواجب عليها ثلاث حيض لو حرةً، أو ثنتان لو أمة بالنص فلا ينتقض عنها.
And she does not count with menses: i.e., if a woman is divorced while she is menstruating, that menstruation in which the divorce occurred is not counted, because what is obligatory upon her is three menses if free, or two if a slave according to the text, so it is not reduced from her.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This is a concise ruling from the matn: when a woman is divorced during her menstruation, that menstruation does not count towards her 'iddah. The reason is that the 'iddah of a free woman is three complete menses, and the menses in which divorce is pronounced is considered incomplete because she was already in that state at the time of divorce. Therefore, she must count the next three menses (or two for a slave woman) after that incomplete one. This is based on the textual evidence that the 'iddah consists of 'three quru' (menstrual cycles), and the cycle that already started at divorce does not fulfill the condition of being a post-divorce cycle. This is a standard Hanafi position and forms part of the memorization anchor for students.

Key Terms
الحيضة (al-ḥayḍah): A single menstrual cycle; in 'iddah, only cycles that occur completely after divorce are counted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave woman is divorced while she is menstruating, how many complete menstruations must she observe for her 'iddah?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Zero

Key Takeaway
The menstruation during which divorce occurs is never counted toward the 'iddah; the count begins from the next complete cycle.

Iddah of the Pregnant Wife of a Minor: The Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on iddah for umm walad and minor's pregnant wife with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position regarding the iddah of a pregnant wife of a minor who dies, and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, identifying the dalil and the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Even if the pregnancy is not attributable to the deceased minor, the iddah ends with delivery.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the iddah of a minor's wife who is pregnant at his death. While all agree on the general rule that a pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery, Abu Yusuf argues that if the pregnancy cannot be from the minor (due to his age), the widow must observe four months and ten days. We will examine the proofs and understand why the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios in the text
The text covers the iddah of the umm walad upon her master's death and the iddah of a minor's pregnant wife. Focus on the latter.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that her iddah ends with delivery, regardless of whether the pregnancy is attributable to the minor.
This is based on the general application of Quran 65:4.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf disagrees because the pregnancy is not of established lineage from the minor, so he treats it as a new event after death, giving a default widow's iddah of four months and ten days.
4
Apply the Quranic dalil and purpose
The majority argue that the Quranic verse is general and the purpose of iddah is to fulfill the rights of marriage, not merely to clear the womb. This purpose exists even if the pregnancy is not from the deceased husband.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأُمُّ الْوَلَدِ الْحَيْضُ لِلْمَوْتِ وَغَيْرِهِ، وَزَوْجَةُ الصَّغِيرِ الْحَامِلِ وَضْعُ الْحَمْلِ. وَالْحَامِلُ بَعْدَهُ الشُّهُورُ وَالنَّسَبُ مُنْتَفٍ فِيهِمَا. وَذَلِكَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ. وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ: عِدَّتُهَا أَرْبَعَةُ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْرًا؛ لِأَنَّ الْحَمْلَ لَيْسَ بِثَابِتِ النَّسَبِ مِنْهُ، فَصَارَ كَالْحَادِثِ بَعْدَ الْمَوْتِ. وَلَهُمَا إِطْلَاقُ قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَأُولَاتُ الْأَحْمَالِ﴾؛ وَلِأَنَّهَا مُقَدَّرَةٌ بِمُدَّةِ وَضْعِ الْحَمْلِ فِي ذَوَاتِ الْأَحْمَالِ، طَالَتِ الْمُدَّةُ أَوْ قَصُرَتْ. لَا لِلتَّعَارُفِ عَنْ فَرَاغِ الرَّحِمِ؛ لِشَرْعِهَا بِالْأَشْهُرِ مَعَ وُجُودِ الْأَقْرَاءِ لِقَضَاءِ حَقِّ النِّكَاحِ، وَهَذَا الْمَعْنَى يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي الصَّبِيِّ؛ لِأَنَّهَا حَامِلٌ مُتَوَفًّى عَنْهَا زَوْجُهَا، فَعَلَيْهَا الْعِدَّةُ بِوَضْعِ الْحَمْلِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الْحَمْلُ مِنْهُ.
And the umm walad (observes iddah by) menses for death and other (causes), and the wife of the minor who is pregnant (observes iddah by) delivery. And the one who becomes pregnant after (his death) (observes iddah by) months, and lineage is negated in both cases. That is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: her iddah is four months and ten days, because the pregnancy is not of established lineage from him, so it becomes like a new occurrence after death. Their proof is the general application of His statement: 'And those who are pregnant...' and because it is determined by the period of delivery for pregnant women, whether the time is long or short. (It is) not for ascertaining the emptiness of the womb, because iddah is prescribed by months even when menses exist, as a fulfillment of the right of marriage. This meaning applies to the minor, because she is a pregnant woman whose husband has died, so she must observe iddah by delivery even if the pregnancy is not from him.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage addresses the iddah of two women: the umm walad (mother of a child upon her master's death) and the wife of a minor who dies while she is pregnant. For the umm walad, the ruling is that her iddah is by menses if she menstruates, and by months if she does not. For the pregnant wife of a minor, her iddah is until delivery, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. They cite the Quranic verse 'And those who are pregnant, their term is when they give birth' (65:4). Abu Yusuf disagrees: because the pregnancy cannot be attributed to the minor (as he is incapable of procreation), the pregnancy is treated as if it occurred after death, so her iddah is the normal four months and ten days for a widow. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that the verse is general and includes all pregnant widows, and the purpose of iddah is not merely to clear the womb but to fulfill the rights of marriage; this purpose is achieved by waiting for delivery regardless of the pregnancy's origin. The text also notes that in both scenarios (pregnancy at death or after death), the lineage from the minor is not established, but the iddah ruling differs.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master; upon her master's death, she becomes free and observes iddah.
وضع الحمل (wad' al-haml): Delivery of the pregnancy; used here as the termination point of iddah for a pregnant woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the iddah of a wife of a minor who dies while she is pregnant?
A) Four months and ten days, because the pregnancy is not attributable to the minor
B) Until she delivers, because the Quranic verse is general
C) Three menstrual cycles, because she is free
D) She does not have to observe iddah because the marriage was invalid

Key Takeaway
A pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery, even if the pregnancy is not from the deceased husband, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.
